<p><b>Jupiter</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Iūpiter</i> <small></small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Latin" title="Help:IPA/Latin">[ˈjuːpɪtɛr]</a></span> or <i>Iuppiter</i> <small></small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Latin" title="Help:IPA/Latin">[ˈjʊppɪtɛr]</a></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[1]</a></sup>, from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Italic_language" title="Proto-Italic language">Proto-Italic</a> *djous "day, sky" + *patēr "father", thus "<a href="/wiki/Sky_father" title="Sky father">sky father</a>"), also known as <b>Jove</b> (<a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">gen</a>. <i>Iovis</i> <small></small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Latin" title="Help:IPA/Latin">[ˈjɔwɪs]</a></span>), was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sky_god" title="Sky god">god of the sky</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/God_of_thunder" title="God of thunder">thunder</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pantheon_(gods)" title="Pantheon (gods)">king of the gods</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_religion" title="Ancient Roman religion">Ancient Roman religion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">mythology</a>. Jupiter was the chief deity of Roman state religion throughout the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Republican</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Imperial</a> eras, until <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great_and_Christianity" title="Constantine the Great and Christianity">Christianity became the dominant religion of the Empire</a>. In Roman mythology, he negotiates with <a href="/wiki/Numa_Pompilius" title="Numa Pompilius">Numa Pompilius</a>, the second <a href="/wiki/King_of_Rome" title="King of Rome">king of Rome</a>, to establish principles of Roman religion such as offering, or sacrifice.
</p><p>Jupiter is usually thought to have originated as an aerial god. His identifying implement is the <a href="/wiki/Thunderbolt" title="Thunderbolt">thunderbolt</a> and his primary sacred animal is the eagle,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[2]</a></sup> which held precedence over other birds in the taking of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auspices" title="Auspices">auspices</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup> and became one of the most common symbols of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_army" title="Roman army">Roman army</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Aquila_(Roman)" title="Aquila (Roman)">Aquila</a>). The two emblems were often combined to represent the god in the form of an eagle holding in its claws a thunderbolt, frequently seen on Greek and Roman coins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup> As the sky-god, he was a divine witness to oaths, the sacred trust on which justice and good government depend. Many of his functions were focused on the <a href="/wiki/Capitoline_Hill" title="Capitoline Hill">Capitoline Hill</a>, where the <a href="/wiki/Arx_(Roman)" title="Arx (Roman)">citadel</a> was located. In the <a href="/wiki/Capitoline_Triad" title="Capitoline Triad">Capitoline Triad</a>, he was the central guardian of the state with <a href="/wiki/Juno_(mythology)" title="Juno (mythology)">Juno</a> and <a href="/wiki/Minerva" title="Minerva">Minerva</a>. His sacred tree was the oak.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_statue,_Vaticana.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2993" data-file-width="2181" decoding="async" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Jupiter_statue%2C_Vaticana.jpg/200px-Jupiter_statue%2C_Vaticana.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Jupiter_statue%2C_Vaticana.jpg/300px-Jupiter_statue%2C_Vaticana.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Jupiter_statue%2C_Vaticana.jpg/400px-Jupiter_statue%2C_Vaticana.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_statue,_Vaticana.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Jupiter, Vatican, Rome.</div></div></div>
<p>The Romans regarded Jupiter as the <a href="/wiki/Interpretatio_graeca" title="Interpretatio graeca">equivalent</a> of the Greek <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lar_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lar-6">[5]</a></sup> and in <a href="/wiki/Latin_literature" title="Latin literature">Latin literature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_art" title="Roman art">Roman art</a>, the myths and iconography of Zeus are adapted under the name <i>Iuppiter</i>. In the Greek-influenced tradition, Jupiter was the brother of <a href="/wiki/Neptune_(mythology)" title="Neptune (mythology)">Neptune</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pluto_(mythology)" title="Pluto (mythology)">Pluto</a>, the Roman equivalents of <a href="/wiki/Poseidon" title="Poseidon">Poseidon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hades" title="Hades">Hades</a> respectively. Each presided over one of the three realms of the universe: sky, the waters, and the underworld. The <a href="/wiki/Italic_peoples" title="Italic peoples">Italic</a> <b>Diespiter</b> was also a sky god who manifested himself in the daylight, usually identified with Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tinia" title="Tinia">Tinia</a> is usually regarded as his <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_religion" title="Etruscan religion">Etruscan</a> counterpart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lens_-_Inauguration_du_Louvre-Lens_le_4_d%C3%A9cembre_2012,_la_Galerie_du_Temps,_n%C2%B0_058.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3299" data-file-width="2736" decoding="async" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Lens_-_Inauguration_du_Louvre-Lens_le_4_d%C3%A9cembre_2012%2C_la_Galerie_du_Temps%2C_n%C2%B0_058.JPG/200px-Lens_-_Inauguration_du_Louvre-Lens_le_4_d%C3%A9cembre_2012%2C_la_Galerie_du_Temps%2C_n%C2%B0_058.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Lens_-_Inauguration_du_Louvre-Lens_le_4_d%C3%A9cembre_2012%2C_la_Galerie_du_Temps%2C_n%C2%B0_058.JPG/300px-Lens_-_Inauguration_du_Louvre-Lens_le_4_d%C3%A9cembre_2012%2C_la_Galerie_du_Temps%2C_n%C2%B0_058.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Lens_-_Inauguration_du_Louvre-Lens_le_4_d%C3%A9cembre_2012%2C_la_Galerie_du_Temps%2C_n%C2%B0_058.JPG/400px-Lens_-_Inauguration_du_Louvre-Lens_le_4_d%C3%A9cembre_2012%2C_la_Galerie_du_Temps%2C_n%C2%B0_058.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lens_-_Inauguration_du_Louvre-Lens_le_4_d%C3%A9cembre_2012,_la_Galerie_du_Temps,_n%C2%B0_058.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Decor Fragment of a triumphal arch: <i> The Emperor's Guards, The <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_Guard" title="Praetorian Guard">Praetorian Guard</a></i>, featured in a <a href="/wiki/Praetorians_Relief" title="Praetorians Relief">relief</a> with an <a href="/wiki/Aquila_(Roman)" title="Aquila (Roman)">eagle</a> grasping a <a href="/wiki/Thunderbolt" title="Thunderbolt">thunderbolt</a> through its claws; in reference to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_religion" title="Ancient Roman religion">Roman</a> <a href="/wiki/Interpretatio_graeca" title="Interpretatio graeca">equivalent</a> form of <i>Jupiter</i>.</div></div></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_in_the_state">Role in the state</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Role in the state">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Romans believed that Jupiter granted them supremacy because they had honoured him more than any other people had. Jupiter was "the fount of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auspices" title="Auspices">auspices</a> upon which the relationship of the city with the gods rested."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> He personified the divine authority of Rome's highest offices, internal organization, and external relations. His image in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Republican</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Imperial</a> Capitol bore <a href="/wiki/Regalia" title="Regalia">regalia</a> associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kings_of_Rome" title="Kings of Rome">Rome's ancient kings</a> and the highest <a href="/wiki/Roman_consul" title="Roman consul">consular</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_cult_(ancient_Rome)" title="Imperial cult (ancient Rome)">Imperial honours</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arch_of_titus_-_imperial_cortege.jpg"><img alt="Bas-relief of Roman driver in four-horse chariot, facing left." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="906" data-file-width="1816" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Arch_of_titus_-_imperial_cortege.jpg/330px-Arch_of_titus_-_imperial_cortege.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Arch_of_titus_-_imperial_cortege.jpg/495px-Arch_of_titus_-_imperial_cortege.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Arch_of_titus_-_imperial_cortege.jpg/660px-Arch_of_titus_-_imperial_cortege.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arch_of_titus_-_imperial_cortege.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Triumphator</i> in his four-horse chariot, from the <a href="/wiki/Arch_of_Titus" title="Arch of Titus">Arch of Titus</a></div></div></div>
<p>The consuls swore their oath of office in Jupiter's name, and honoured him on the annual <i><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_ancient_Roman_religion#feria" title="Glossary of ancient Roman religion">feriae</a></i> of the Capitol in September. To thank him for his help (and to secure his continued support), they offered him a white ox <i>(bos mas)</i> with gilded horns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> A similar offering was made by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Triumph" title="Roman Triumph">triumphal generals</a>, who surrendered the tokens of their victory at the feet of Jupiter's statue in the Capitol. Some scholars have viewed the <i>triumphator</i> as embodying (or impersonating) Jupiter in the triumphal procession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jupiter's association with kingship and sovereignty was reinterpreted as Rome's form of government changed. Originally, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kings_of_Rome" title="Kings of Rome">Rome was ruled by kings</a>; after the monarchy was abolished and the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Republic</a> established, religious prerogatives were transferred to the <i>patres</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Patrician_(ancient_Rome)" title="Patrician (ancient Rome)">patrician ruling class</a>. Nostalgia for the kingship <i>(affectatio regni)</i> was considered treasonous. Those suspected of harbouring monarchical ambitions were punished, regardless of their service to the state. In the 5th century BC, the <i>triumphator</i> <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Furius_Camillus" title="Marcus Furius Camillus">Camillus</a> was sent into exile after he drove a chariot with a team of four white horses <i>(<a href="/wiki/Quadriga" title="Quadriga">quadriga</a>)</i>—an honour reserved for Jupiter himself. When <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Manlius_Capitolinus" title="Marcus Manlius Capitolinus">Marcus Manlius</a>, whose defense of the Capitol against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sack_of_Rome_(387_BC)" title="Sack of Rome (387 BC)">invading Gauls</a> had earned him the name <i>Capitolinus</i>, was accused of regal pretensions, he was executed as a traitor by being cast from the <a href="/wiki/Tarpeian_Rock" title="Tarpeian Rock">Tarpeian Rock</a>. His house on the Capitoline Hill was razed, and it was decreed that no patrician should ever be allowed to live there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> Capitoline Jupiter found himself in a delicate position: he represented a continuity of royal power from the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Kingdom" title="Roman Kingdom">Regal period</a>, and conferred power on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Magistrates" title="Roman Magistrates">magistrates</a> who paid their respects to him; at the same time he embodied that which was now forbidden, abhorred, and scorned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Conflict_of_the_Orders" title="Conflict of the Orders">Conflict of the Orders</a>, Rome's <a href="/wiki/Plebs" title="Plebs">plebeians</a> demanded the right to hold political and religious office. During their first <i><a href="/wiki/Secessio_plebis" title="Secessio plebis">secessio</a></i> (similar to a <a href="/wiki/General_strike" title="General strike">general strike</a>), they withdrew from the city and threatened to found their own. When they agreed to come back to Rome they vowed the hill where they had retreated to Jupiter as symbol and guarantor of the unity of the Roman <i>res publica</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> Plebeians eventually became eligible for all the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Magistrates" title="Roman Magistrates">magistracies</a> and most priesthoods, but the high priest of Jupiter <i>(<a href="/wiki/Flamen_Dialis" title="Flamen Dialis">Flamen Dialis</a>)</i> remained the preserve of patricians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flamen_and_Flaminica_Dialis">Flamen and Flaminica Dialis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Flamen and Flaminica Dialis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ara_pacis_fregio_lato_ovest_2_B.JPG"><img alt="Bas-relief of five Roman priests" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="371" data-file-width="515" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Ara_pacis_fregio_lato_ovest_2_B.JPG/220px-Ara_pacis_fregio_lato_ovest_2_B.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Ara_pacis_fregio_lato_ovest_2_B.JPG/330px-Ara_pacis_fregio_lato_ovest_2_B.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Ara_pacis_fregio_lato_ovest_2_B.JPG/440px-Ara_pacis_fregio_lato_ovest_2_B.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ara_pacis_fregio_lato_ovest_2_B.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detail of <a href="/wiki/Relief" title="Relief">relief</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Ara_Pacis" title="Ara Pacis">Augustan Altar of Peace</a>, showing <i>flamines</i> wearing the pointed <i><a href="/wiki/Apex_(headdress)" title="Apex (headdress)">apex</a></i></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Flamen_Dialis" title="Flamen Dialis">Flamen Dialis</a></div>
<p>Jupiter was served by the patrician Flamen Dialis, the highest-ranking member of the <i><a href="/wiki/Flamen" title="Flamen">flamines</a>,</i> a <a href="/wiki/Collegium_(ancient_Rome)" title="Collegium (ancient Rome)">college</a> of fifteen priests in the official public cult of Rome, each of whom was devoted to a particular deity. His wife, the Flaminica Dialis, had her own duties, and presided over the sacrifice of a ram to Jupiter on each of the <i><a href="/wiki/Roman_calendar#Nundinal_cycle" title="Roman calendar">nundinae</a></i>, the "market" days of a calendar cycle, comparable to a week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> The couple were required to marry by the exclusive patrician ritual <i><a href="/wiki/Confarreatio" title="Confarreatio">confarreatio</a></i>, which included a sacrifice of <a href="/wiki/Spelt" title="Spelt">spelt</a> bread to Jupiter Farreus (from <i>far</i>, "wheat, grain").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>The office of Flamen Dialis was circumscribed by several unique ritual prohibitions, some of which shed light on the sovereign nature of the god himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> For instance, the <i>flamen</i> may remove his clothes or <i><a href="/wiki/Apex_(headdress)" title="Apex (headdress)">apex</a></i> (his pointed hat) only when under a roof, in order to avoid showing himself naked to the sky—that is, "as if under the eyes of Jupiter" as god of the heavens. Every time the Flaminica saw a lightning bolt or heard a clap of thunder (Jupiter's distinctive instrument), she was prohibited from carrying on with her normal routine until she placated the god.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some privileges of the <i>flamen</i> of Jupiter may reflect the regal nature of Jupiter: he had the use of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curule_chair" title="Curule chair">curule chair</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> and was the only priest <i>(<a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_ancient_Roman_religion#sacerdos" title="Glossary of ancient Roman religion">sacerdos</a>)</i> who was preceded by a <a href="/wiki/Lictor" title="Lictor">lictor</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> and had a seat in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_senate" title="Roman senate">senate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> Other regulations concern his ritual purity and his separation from the military function; he was forbidden to ride a horse or see the army outside the sacred boundary of Rome <i>(<a href="/wiki/Pomerium" title="Pomerium">pomerium</a>).</i> Although he served the god who embodied the sanctity of the oath, it was not religiously permissible <i>(<a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_ancient_Roman_religion#fas" title="Glossary of ancient Roman religion">fas</a>)</i> for the Dialis to swear an oath.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> He could not have contacts with anything dead or connected with death: corpses, funerals, funeral fires, raw meat. This set of restrictions reflects the fulness of life and absolute freedom that are features of Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Augurs">Augurs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Augurs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i>augures publici</i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augurs" title="Augurs">augurs</a> were a college of <i>sacerdotes</i> who were in charge of all inaugurations and of the performing of ceremonies known as <i>auguria</i>. Their creation was traditionally ascribed to <a href="/wiki/Romulus" title="Romulus">Romulus</a>. They were considered the only official interpreters of Jupiter's will, thence they were essential to the very existence of the Roman State as Romans saw in Jupiter the only source of state authority.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fetials">Fetials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Fetials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fetials" title="Fetials">fetials</a> were a college of 20 men devoted to the religious administration of international affairs of state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> Their task was to preserve and apply the fetial law <i>(ius fetiale)</i>, a complex set of procedures aimed at ensuring the protection of the gods in Rome's relations with foreign states. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iuppiter_Lapis" title="Iuppiter Lapis">Iuppiter Lapis</a> is the god under whose protection they act, and whom the chief fetial <i>(pater patratus)</i> invokes in the rite concluding a treaty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> If a <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_war" title="Declaration of war">declaration of war</a> ensues, the fetial calls upon Jupiter and <a href="/wiki/Quirinus" title="Quirinus">Quirinus</a>, the heavenly, earthly and <a href="/wiki/Chthonic" title="Chthonic">chthonic</a> gods as witnesses of any potential violation of the <i>ius</i>. He can then declare war within 33 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>The action of the fetials falls under Jupiter's jurisdiction as the divine defender of good faith. Several emblems of the fetial office pertain to Jupiter. The <i>silex</i> was the stone used for the fetial sacrifice, housed in the Temple of <a href="/wiki/Feretrius" title="Feretrius">Iuppiter Feretrius</a>, as was their sceptre. Sacred herbs <i>(sagmina)</i>, sometimes identified as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vervain" title="Vervain">vervain</a>, had to be taken from the nearby <a href="/wiki/Arx_(Roman)" title="Arx (Roman)">citadel <i>(arx)</i></a> for their ritual use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jupiter_and_religion_in_the_secessions_of_the_plebs">Jupiter and religion in the secessions of the plebs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Jupiter and religion in the secessions of the plebs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_cameo_Louvre_Bj1820.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2440" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="336" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Jupiter_cameo_Louvre_Bj1820.jpg/220px-Jupiter_cameo_Louvre_Bj1820.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Jupiter_cameo_Louvre_Bj1820.jpg/330px-Jupiter_cameo_Louvre_Bj1820.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Jupiter_cameo_Louvre_Bj1820.jpg/440px-Jupiter_cameo_Louvre_Bj1820.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_cameo_Louvre_Bj1820.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jupiter's head crowned with laurel and ivy. Sardonyx cameo (<a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>The role of Jupiter in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conflict_of_the_orders" title="Conflict of the orders">conflict of the orders</a> is a reflection of the religiosity of the Romans. On one side, the patricians were able to naturally claim the support of the supreme god as they held the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auspices" title="Auspices">auspices</a> of the State. On the other side, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pleb" title="Pleb">plebs</a> (plebeians) argued that, as Jupiter was the source of justice, they had his favor because their cause was just.
</p><p>The first secession was caused by the excessive debt burden on the plebs. The legal institute of the <i><a href="/wiki/Nexum" title="Nexum">nexum</a></i> permitted a debtor to become a slave of his creditor. The plebs argued the debts had become unsustainable because of the expenses of the wars wanted by the patricians. As the senate did not accede to the proposal of a total debt remission advanced by dictator and augur <a href="/wiki/Manius_Valerius_Maximus" title="Manius Valerius Maximus">Manius Valerius Maximus</a> the plebs retired on the Mount Sacer, a hill located three Roman miles to the North-northeast of Rome, past the Nomentan bridge on river <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anio" title="Anio">Anio</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> The place is windy and was usually the site of rites of divination performed by haruspices. The senate in the end sent a delegation composed of ten members with full powers of making a deal with the plebs, of which were part <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Menenius_Agrippa" title="Menenius Agrippa">Menenius Agrippa</a> and Manius Valerius. It was Valerius, according to the inscription found at Arezzo in 1688 and written on the order of Augustus as well as other literary sources, that brought the plebs down from the Mount, after the secessionists had consecrated it to <i>Jupiter Territor</i> and built an altar (<i>ara</i>) on its summit. The fear of the wrath of Jupiter was an important element in the solution of the crisis. The consecration of the Mount probably referred to its summit only. The ritual requested the participation of both an augur (presumably Manius Valerius himself) and a pontifex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>The second secession was caused by the autocratic and arrogant behaviour of the <i><a href="/wiki/Decemviri" title="Decemviri">decemviri</a></i>, who had been charged by the Roman people with writing down the laws in use till then kept secret by the patrician magistrates and the <i>sacerdotes</i>. All magistracies and the tribunes of the plebs had resigned in advance. The task resulted in the XII Tables, which though concerned only private law. The plebs once again retreated to the Sacer Mons: this act besides recalling the first secession was meant to seek the protection of the supreme god. The secession ended with the resignation of the <i>decemviri</i> and an amnesty for the rebellious soldiers who had deserted from their camp near Mount Algidus while warring against the Volscians, abandoning the commanders. The amnesty was granted by the senate and guaranteed by the <i>pontifex maximus</i> Quintus Furius (in Livy's version) (or Marcus Papirius) who also supervised the nomination of the new tribunes of the plebs, then gathered on the Aventine Hill. The role played by the <i>pontifex maximus</i> in a situation of vacation of powers is a significant element underlining the religious basis and character of the <i>tribunicia potestas</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Myths_and_legends">Myths and legends</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Myths and legends">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zeus_pompei.JPG"><img alt="Painting of a bearded, seated Jupiter, unclothed from the waist up and holding a staff" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2588" data-file-width="1675" decoding="async" height="340" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Zeus_pompei.JPG/220px-Zeus_pompei.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Zeus_pompei.JPG/330px-Zeus_pompei.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Zeus_pompei.JPG/440px-Zeus_pompei.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zeus_pompei.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jupiter in a wall painting from <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a>, with eagle and globe</div></div></div>
<p>A dominant line of scholarship has held that Rome lacked a body of myths in its earliest period, or that this original mythology has been irrecoverably obscured by the influence of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek narrative tradition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Hellenization" title="Hellenization">Hellenization</a> of Roman culture, Latin literature and iconography reinterpreted the myths of Zeus in depictions and narratives of Jupiter. In the legendary history of Rome, Jupiter is often connected to kings and kingship.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth">Birth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Birth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Jupiter is depicted as the twin of Juno in a statue at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Praeneste" title="Praeneste">Praeneste</a> that showed them nursed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fortuna_Primigenia" title="Fortuna Primigenia">Fortuna Primigenia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> An inscription that is also from Praeneste, however, says that Fortuna Primigenia was Jupiter's first-born child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup>  Jacqueline Champeaux sees this contradiction as the result of successive different cultural and religious phases, in which a wave of influence coming from the Hellenic world made Fortuna the daughter of Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> The childhood of Zeus is an important theme in Greek religion, art and literature, but there are only rare (or dubious) depictions of Jupiter as a child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Numa">Numa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Numa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Faced by a period of bad weather endangering the harvest during one early spring, King <a href="/wiki/Numa_Pompilius" title="Numa Pompilius">Numa</a> resorted to the scheme of asking the advice of the god by evoking his presence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> He succeeded through the help of Picus and Faunus, whom he had imprisoned by making them drunk. The two gods (with a charm) evoked Jupiter, who was forced to come down to earth at the Aventine (hence named <i>Iuppiter Elicius</i>, according to Ovid). After Numa skilfully avoided the requests of the god for human sacrifices, Jupiter agreed to his request to know how lightning bolts are averted, asking only for the substitutions Numa had mentioned: an onion bulb, hairs and a fish. Moreover, Jupiter promised that at the sunrise of the following day he would give to Numa and the Roman people pawns of the <i>imperium</i>. The following day, after throwing three lightning bolts across a clear sky, Jupiter sent down from heaven a shield. Since this shield had no angles, Numa named it <i>ancile</i>; because in it resided the fate of the <i>imperium</i>, he had many copies made of it to disguise the real one. He asked the smith <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mamurius_Veturius" title="Mamurius Veturius">Mamurius Veturius</a> to make the copies, and gave them to the <a href="/wiki/Salii" title="Salii">Salii</a>. As his only reward, Mamurius expressed the wish that his name be sung in the last of their <i>carmina</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> Plutarch gives a slightly different version of the story, writing that the cause of the miraculous drop of the shield was a plague and not linking it with the Roman <i>imperium</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tullus_Hostilius">Tullus Hostilius</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Tullus Hostilius">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Throughout his reign, <a href="/wiki/Tullus_Hostilius" title="Tullus Hostilius">King Tullus</a> had a scornful attitude towards religion. His temperament was warlike, and he disregarded religious rites and piety. After conquering the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albani_people" title="Albani people">Albans</a> with the duel between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horatii_and_Curiatii" title="Horatii and Curiatii">Horatii and Curiatii</a>, Tullus destroyed <a href="/wiki/Alba_Longa" title="Alba Longa">Alba Longa</a> and deported its inhabitants to Rome. As <a href="/wiki/Livy" title="Livy">Livy</a> tells the story, omens <i>(<a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_ancient_Roman_religion#prodigium" title="Glossary of ancient Roman religion">prodigia</a>)</i> in the form of a rain of stones occurred on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alban_Mount" title="Alban Mount">Alban Mount</a> because the deported Albans had disregarded their ancestral rites linked to the sanctuary of Jupiter. In addition to the omens, a voice was heard requesting that the Albans perform the rites. A plague followed and at last the king himself fell ill. As a consequence, the warlike character of Tullus broke down; he resorted to religion and petty, superstitious practices. At last, he found a book by Numa recording a secret rite on how to evoke <i>Iuppiter Elicius</i>. The king attempted to perform it, but since he executed the rite improperly the god threw a lightning bolt which burned down the king's house and killed Tullus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tarquin_the_Elder">Tarquin the Elder</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Tarquin the Elder">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When approaching Rome (where Tarquin was heading to try his luck in politics after unsuccessful attempts in his native <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tarquinii" title="Tarquinii">Tarquinii</a>), an eagle swooped down, removed his hat, flew screaming in circles, replaced the hat on his head and flew away.  Tarquin's wife <a href="/wiki/Tanaquil" title="Tanaquil">Tanaquil</a> interpreted this as a sign that he would become king based on the bird, the quadrant of the sky from which it came, the god who had sent it and the fact it touched his hat (an item of clothing placed on a man's most noble part, the head).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Elder Tarquin is credited with introducing the Capitoline Triad to Rome, by building the so-called Capitolium Vetus. Macrobius writes this issued from his Samothracian mystery beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cult">Cult</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Cult">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marcusaureliuspannel.jpg"><img alt="Bas=relief of family group, with an animal, outside large building with columns" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="565" decoding="async" height="312" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Marcusaureliuspannel.jpg/220px-Marcusaureliuspannel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Marcusaureliuspannel.jpg/330px-Marcusaureliuspannel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Marcusaureliuspannel.jpg/440px-Marcusaureliuspannel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marcusaureliuspannel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Emperor <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a>, attended by his family, offers sacrifice outside the Temple of Jupiter Capitolinus after his victories in Germany (late 2nd century AD). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capitoline_Museum" title="Capitoline Museum">Capitoline Museum</a>, Rome</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sacrifices">Sacrifices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Sacrifices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sacrificial victims (<i><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_ancient_Roman_religion#hostia" title="Glossary of ancient Roman religion">hostiae</a></i>) offered to Jupiter were the ox (castrated bull), the lamb  (on the Ides, the  <i>ovis idulis</i>) and the <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/wether" title="wikt:wether">wether</a> (on the Ides of January).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup>  The animals were required to be white. The question of the lamb's gender is unresolved; while a lamb is generally male, for the vintage-opening festival the flamen Dialis sacrificed a <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">ewe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup> This rule seems to have had many exceptions, as the sacrifice of a ram on the <a href="/wiki/Nundinae" title="Nundinae">Nundinae</a> by the <i>flaminica Dialis</i> demonstrates.
During one of the crises of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Punic_War" title="Second Punic War">Punic Wars</a>, Jupiter was offered every animal born that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temples">Temples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Temples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Temple_of_Capitoline_Jupiter">Temple of Capitoline Jupiter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Temple of Capitoline Jupiter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temple_of_Jupiter_(Capitoline_Hill)" title="Temple of Jupiter (Capitoline Hill)">Temple of Jupiter (Capitoline Hill)</a></div>
<p>The temple to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temple_of_Jupiter_(Capitoline_Hill)" title="Temple of Jupiter (Capitoline Hill)">Jupiter Optimus Maximus</a> stood on the <a href="/wiki/Capitoline_Hill" title="Capitoline Hill">Capitoline Hill</a> in Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> Jupiter was worshiped there as an individual deity, and with <a href="/wiki/Juno_(mythology)" title="Juno (mythology)">Juno</a> and <a href="/wiki/Minerva" title="Minerva">Minerva</a> as part of the <a href="/wiki/Capitoline_Triad" title="Capitoline Triad">Capitoline Triad</a>. The building was supposedly begun by king <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tarquinius_Priscus" title="Tarquinius Priscus">Tarquinius Priscus</a>, completed by the last king (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tarquinius_Superbus" title="Tarquinius Superbus">Tarquinius Superbus</a>) and inaugurated in the early days of the Roman Republic (September 13, 509 BC). It was topped with the statues of four horses drawing a <a href="/wiki/Quadriga" title="Quadriga">quadriga</a>, with Jupiter as charioteer. A large statue of Jupiter stood within; on festival days, its face was painted red. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> In (or near) this temple was the <i>Iuppiter Lapis</i>: the <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_Stone" title="Jupiter Stone">Jupiter Stone</a>, on which oaths could be sworn.
</p><p>Jupiter's Capitoline Temple probably served as the architectural model for his provincial temples. 
When Hadrian built <a href="/wiki/Aelia_Capitolina" title="Aelia Capitolina">Aelia Capitolina</a> on the site of <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>, a temple to Jupiter Capitolinus was erected in the place of the destroyed <a href="/wiki/Temple_in_Jerusalem" title="Temple in Jerusalem">Temple in Jerusalem</a>. 
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_temples_in_Rome">Other temples in Rome</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Other temples in Rome">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There were two temples in Rome dedicated to <i>Iuppiter Stator</i>; <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Jupiter_Stator_(8th_century_BC)" title="Temple of Jupiter Stator (8th century BC)">the first one</a> was built and dedicated in 294 BC by <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Atilius_Regulus" title="Marcus Atilius Regulus">Marcus Atilius Regulus</a> after the third Samnite War. It was located on the <i>Via Nova</i>, below the <i>Porta Mugonia</i>, ancient entrance to the Palatine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> Legend has attributed its founding to Romulus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> There may have been an earlier shrine <i>(<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fanum" title="Fanum">fanum</a>)</i>, since the Jupiter's cult is attested epigraphically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a> places the temple's dedication on June 27, but it is unclear whether this was the original date,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> or the rededication after the restoration by Augustus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Weihealtar_f%C3%BCr_Jupiter.jpg"><img alt="Narrow stone altar, with inscription" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3436" data-file-width="2302" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Weihealtar_f%C3%BCr_Jupiter.jpg/170px-Weihealtar_f%C3%BCr_Jupiter.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Weihealtar_f%C3%BCr_Jupiter.jpg/255px-Weihealtar_f%C3%BCr_Jupiter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Weihealtar_f%C3%BCr_Jupiter.jpg/340px-Weihealtar_f%C3%BCr_Jupiter.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Weihealtar_f%C3%BCr_Jupiter.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Altar to Jupiter on the outskirts of legionary fortress, 2nd–3rd century AD. Inscription: "Dedicated by L. Lollius Clarus for himself and his family"</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Jupiter_Stator_(2nd_century_BC)" title="Temple of Jupiter Stator (2nd century BC)">A second temple of <i>Iuppiter Stator</i></a> was built and dedicated by Quintus Caecilus Metellus Macedonicus after his triumph in 146 BC near the <a href="/wiki/Circus_Flaminius" title="Circus Flaminius">Circus Flaminius</a>. It was connected to <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Juno_Regina_(Campus_Martius)" title="Temple of Juno Regina (Campus Martius)">the restored temple of <i>Iuno Regina</i></a> with a <a href="/wiki/Portico" title="Portico">portico</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Porticus_Octaviae" title="Porticus Octaviae">porticus Metelli</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Iuppiter Victor</i> had a temple dedicated by <a href="/wiki/Quintus_Fabius_Maximus_Gurges_(consul_292_BC)" title="Quintus Fabius Maximus Gurges (consul 292 BC)">Quintus Fabius Maximus Gurges</a> during the third Samnite War in 295 BC. Its location is unknown, but it may be on the Quirinal, on which an inscription reading <i>D]iovei Victore</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> has been found, or on the Palatine according to the <i>Notitia</i> in the <i>Liber Regionum</i> (regio X), which reads: <i>aedes Iovis Victoris</i>. Either might have been dedicated on April 13 or June 13 (days of <i>Iuppiter Victor</i> and of <i>Iuppiter Invictus</i>, respectively, in Ovid's <i><a href="/wiki/Fasti_(poem)" title="Fasti (poem)">Fasti</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup>
Inscriptions from the imperial age have revealed the existence of an otherwise-unknown temple of  <i>Iuppiter Propugnator</i> on the Palatine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iuppiter_Latiaris_and_Feriae_Latinae">Iuppiter Latiaris and Feriae Latinae</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Iuppiter Latiaris and Feriae Latinae">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The cult of <i>Iuppiter Latiaris</i> was the most ancient known cult of the god: it was practised since very remote times near the top of the <i>Mons Albanus</i> on which the god was venerated as the high protector of the Latin League under the hegemony of <a href="/wiki/Alba_Longa" title="Alba Longa">Alba Longa</a>.
</p><p>After the destruction of Alba by king Tullus Hostilius the cult was forsaken.  The god manifested his discontent through the prodigy of a rain of stones: the commission sent by the Roman senate to inquire was also greeted by a rain of stones and heard a loud voice from the grove on the summit of the mount requesting the Albans perform the religious service to the god according to the rites of their country. In consequence of this event the Romans instituted a festival of nine days (<i>nundinae</i>).   Nonetheless a plague ensued:  in the end Tullus Hostilius himself was affected and lastly killed by the god with a lightning bolt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> The festival was reestablished on its primitive site by the last Roman king Tarquin the Proud under the leadership of Rome.
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Feriae_Latinae" title="Feriae Latinae">feriae Latinae</a></i>, or <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Latiar&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Latiar (page does not exist)">Latiar</a></i> as they were known originally,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup> were the common festival  (<i>panegyris</i>) of the so-called Priscan Latins<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> and of the Albans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> Their restoration aimed at grounding Roman hegemony in this ancestral religious tradition of the Latins. The original cult was reinstated unchanged as is testified by some archaic features of the ritual:  the exclusion of wine from the sacrifice<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup> the offers of milk and cheese and the ritual use of rocking among the games. Rocking is one of the most ancient rites mimicking ascent to Heaven and is very widespread. At the <i>Latiar</i> the rocking took place on a tree and the winner was of course the one who had swung the highest. This rite was said to have been instituted by the Albans to commemorate the disappearance of king <a href="/wiki/Latinus" title="Latinus">Latinus</a>, in the battle against <a href="/wiki/Mezentius" title="Mezentius">Mezentius</a> king of <a href="/wiki/Caere" title="Caere">Caere</a>: the rite symbolised a search for him both on earth and in heaven. The rocking as well as the customary drinking of milk was also considered to commemorate and ritually reinstate infancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> The Romans in the last form of the rite brought the sacrificial ox from Rome and every participant was bestowed a portion of the meat, rite known as <i>carnem petere</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> Other games were held in every participant borough. In Rome a race of chariots (<i>quadrigae</i>) was held starting from the Capitol: the winner drank a liquor made with absynth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> This competition has been compared to the Vedic rite of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vajapeya" title="Vajapeya">vajapeya</a>: in it seventeen chariots run a phoney race which must be won by the king in order to allow him to drink a cup of <i>madhu</i>, i. e. <i>soma</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> The feasting lasted for at least four days, possibly six according to <a href="/wiki/Niebuhr" title="Niebuhr">Niebuhr</a>, one day for each of the six Latin and Alban <i>decuriae</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> According to different records 47 or 53 boroughs took part in the festival  (the listed names too differ in Pliny NH III 69 and Dionysius of Halicarnassus AR V 61). The <i>Latiar</i> became an important feature of Roman political life as they were <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feriae_conceptivae" title="Feriae conceptivae">feriae conceptivae</a></i>, i. e. their date varied each year: the consuls and the highest magistrates were required to attend shortly after the beginning of the administration, originally on the Ides of March: the Feriae usually took place in early April. They could not start campaigning before its end and if any part of the games had been neglected or performed unritually the <i>Latiar</i> had to be wholly repeated. The inscriptions from the imperial age record the festival back to the time of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decemvir" title="Decemvir">decemvirs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup>
Wissowa remarks the inner linkage of the temple of the Mons Albanus with that of the Capitol apparent in the common association with the rite of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_triumph" title="Roman triumph">triumph</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWissowa1912110_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWissowa1912110-69">[68]</a></sup> since 231 BC some triumphing commanders had triumphed there first with the same legal features as in Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_calendar">Religious calendar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Religious calendar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Roman_calendar" title="Roman calendar">Roman calendar</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ides">Ides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Ides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ides_(calendar)" title="Ides (calendar)">Ides</a> (the midpoint of the month, with a full moon) was sacred to Jupiter, because on that day heavenly light shone day and night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> Some (or all) Ides were <i><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_ancient_Roman_religion#feria" title="Glossary of ancient Roman religion">Feriae</a> Iovis</i>, sacred to Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> On the Ides, a white lamb (<i>ovis idulis</i>) was led along Rome's <a href="/wiki/Via_Sacra" title="Via Sacra">Sacred Way</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Arx_(Roman)" title="Arx (Roman)">Capitoline Citadel</a> and sacrificed to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> Jupiter's two <i><a href="/wiki/Epulum_Jovis" title="Epulum Jovis">epula Iovis</a></i> festivals fell on the Ides, as did his temple foundation rites as <i>Optimus Maximus</i>, <i>Victor</i>, <i>Invictus</i> and (possibly) <i>Stator</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nundinae">Nundinae</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Nundinae">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Nundinae" title="Nundinae">nundinae</a></i> recurred every ninth day, dividing the calendar into a market cycle analogous to a week. Market days gave rural people <i>(<a href="/wiki/Pagus" title="Pagus">pagi</a>)</i> the opportunity to sell in town and to be informed of religious and political edicts, which were posted publicly for three days. According to tradition, these festival days were instituted by the king <a href="/wiki/Servius_Tullius" title="Servius Tullius">Servius Tullius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> The high priestess of Jupiter <i>(<a href="/wiki/Flamen_Dialis#Flaminica_Dialis" title="Flamen Dialis">Flaminica Dialis</a>)</i> sanctified the days by sacrificing a ram to Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Festivals">Festivals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Festivals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Roman_festivals" title="Roman festivals">Roman festivals</a></div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Republican era</a>, more <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_ancient_Roman_religion#feria" title="Glossary of ancient Roman religion">fixed holidays</a> on the Roman calendar were devoted to Jupiter than to any other deity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Viniculture_and_wine">Viniculture and wine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Viniculture and wine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Festivals of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viniculture" title="Viniculture">viniculture</a> and wine were devoted to Jupiter, since grapes were particularly susceptible to adverse weather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> Dumézil describes wine as a "kingly" drink with the power to inebriate and exhilarate, analogous to the Vedic <a href="/wiki/Soma_(deity)" title="Soma (deity)">Soma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>Three Roman festivals were connected with viniculture and wine.
</p><p>The rustic <i><a href="/wiki/Vinalia" title="Vinalia">Vinalia</a> altera</i> on August 19 asked for good weather for ripening the grapes before harvest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> When the grapes were ripe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> a sheep was sacrificed to Jupiter and the <i>flamen Dialis</i> cut the first of the grape harvest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Meditrinalia" title="Meditrinalia">Meditrinalia</a> on October 11 marked the end of the grape harvest; the new wine was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pressed_(wine)" title="Pressed (wine)">pressed</a>, tasted and mixed with old wine<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup> to control fermentation. In the <i>Fasti Amiternini</i>, this festival is assigned to Jupiter. Later Roman sources invented a goddess <i>Meditrina</i>, probably to explain the name of the festival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the <i><a href="/wiki/Vinalia" title="Vinalia">Vinalia</a> urbana</i> on April 23, new wine was offered to Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> Large quantities of it were poured into a ditch near the temple of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venus_Erycina" title="Venus Erycina">Venus Erycina</a>, which was located on the Capitol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Regifugium_and_Poplifugium">Regifugium and Poplifugium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Regifugium and Poplifugium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Regifugium" title="Regifugium">Regifugium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Poplifugia" title="Poplifugia">Poplifugia</a></div>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Regifugium" title="Regifugium">Regifugium</a></i> ("King's Flight")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> on February 24 has often been discussed in connection with the <i><a href="/wiki/Poplifugia" title="Poplifugia">Poplifugia</a></i> on July 5, a day holy to Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup>  The <i>Regifugium</i> followed the festival of <i>Iuppiter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terminus_(mythology)" title="Terminus (mythology)">Terminus</a></i> (Jupiter of Boundaries) on February 23. Later Roman <a href="/wiki/Antiquarian" title="Antiquarian">antiquarians</a> misinterpreted the <i>Regifugium</i> as marking the expulsion of the monarchy, but the "king" of this festival may have been the priest known as the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rex_sacrorum" title="Rex sacrorum">rex sacrorum</a></i> who ritually enacted the waning and renewal of power associated with the <a href="/wiki/New_Year" title="New Year">New Year</a> (March 1 in the old Roman calendar).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> A temporary vacancy of power (construed as a yearly "<a href="/wiki/Interregnum" title="Interregnum">interregnum</a>") occurred between the <i>Regifugium</i> on February 24 and the New Year on March 1 (when the lunar cycle was thought to coincide again with the solar cycle), and the uncertainty and change during the two winter months were over.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup> Some scholars emphasize the traditional political significance of the day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Poplifugia</i> ("Routing of Armies"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup>), a day sacred to Jupiter, may similarly mark the second half of the year; before the <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar reform</a>, the months were named numerically, <i><a href="/wiki/Quintilis" title="Quintilis">Quintilis</a></i> (the fifth month) to <i>December</i> (the tenth month).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> The <i>Poplifugia</i> was a "primitive military ritual" for which the adult male population assembled for purification rites, after which they ritually dispelled foreign invaders from Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Epula_Iovis">Epula Iovis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Epula Iovis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Epulum_Jovis" title="Epulum Jovis">Epulum Jovis</a></div>
<p>There were two festivals called <i>epulum Iovis</i> ("Feast of Jove"). One was held on September 13, the anniversary of the foundation of Jupiter's Capitoline temple. The other (and probably older) festival was part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plebeian_Games" title="Plebeian Games">Plebeian Games</a> <i>(Ludi Plebei)</i>, and was held on November 13.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> In the 3rd century BC, the <i>epulum Iovis</i> became similar to a <a href="/wiki/Lectisternium" title="Lectisternium">lectisternium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ludi">Ludi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Ludi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ludi" title="Ludi">Ludi</a></div>
<p>The most ancient Roman games followed after one day (considered a <i>dies ater</i>, or "black day", i. e. a day which was traditionally considered unfortunate even though it was not <i>nefas</i>, see also article <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_ancient_Roman_religion" title="Glossary of ancient Roman religion">Glossary of ancient Roman religion</a>) the two <i>Epula Iovis</i> of September and November.
</p><p>The games of September were named <i>Ludi Magni</i>; originally they were not held every year, but later became the annual <i>Ludi Romani</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> and were held in the <a href="/wiki/Circus_Maximus" title="Circus Maximus">Circus Maximus</a> after a procession from the Capitol. The games were attributed to Tarquinius Priscus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup> and linked to the cult of Jupiter on the Capitol. Romans themselves acknowledged analogies with the <a href="/wiki/Roman_triumph" title="Roman triumph">triumph</a>, which Dumézil thinks can be explained by their common Etruscan origin; the magistrate in charge of the games dressed as the <i>triumphator</i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Pompa_circensis" title="Pompa circensis">pompa circensis</a></i> resembled a triumphal procession. Wissowa and Mommsen argue that they were a detached part of the triumph on the above grounds<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup> (a conclusion which Dumézil rejects).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Ludi Plebei</i> took place in November in the <a href="/wiki/Circus_Flaminius" title="Circus Flaminius">Circus Flaminius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Mommsen" title="Mommsen">Mommsen</a> argued that the <i>epulum</i> of the Ludi Plebei was the model of the Ludi Romani, but Wissowa finds the evidence for this assumption insufficient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup> The <i>Ludi Plebei</i> were probably established in 534 BC. Their association with the cult of Jupiter is attested by Cicero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Larentalia">Larentalia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Larentalia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <i>feriae</i> of December 23 were devoted to a major ceremony in honour of <a href="/wiki/Acca_Larentia" title="Acca Larentia">Acca Larentia</a> (or <i>Larentina</i>), in which some of the highest religious authorities participated (probably including the <a href="/wiki/Flamen_Quirinalis" title="Flamen Quirinalis">Flamen Quirinalis</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pontiff" title="Pontiff">pontiffs</a>). The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fasti_Praenestini" title="Fasti Praenestini">Fasti Praenestini</a> marks the day as <i>feriae Iovis</i>, as does Macrobius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup> It is unclear whether the rite of <i>parentatio</i> was itself the reason for the festival of Jupiter, or if this was another festival which happened to fall on the same day. Wissowa denies their association, since Jupiter and his <i>flamen</i> would not be involved with the <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">underworld</a> or the deities of death (or be present at a funeral rite held at a gravesite).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name_and_epithets">Name and epithets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Name and epithets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Latin name <i>Iuppiter</i> originated as a <a href="/wiki/Vocative_case" title="Vocative case">vocative compound</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Latin" title="Old Latin">Old Latin</a> vocative *<i>Iou</i> and <i>pater</i> ("father") and came to replace the Old Latin <a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">nominative case</a> *<i>Ious</i>. Jove<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> is a less common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_(language)" title="English (language)">English</a> formation based on <i>Iov-</i>, the stem of oblique cases of the Latin name. <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">Linguistic</a> studies identify the form *<i>Iou-pater</i> as deriving from the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Indo-European</a> vocative compound *<i>Dyēu-pəter</i> (meaning "O Father Sky-god"; nominative: *<i><a href="/wiki/Dyeus" title="Dyeus">Dyēus</a>-pətēr</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Puteal_de_la_Moncloa_(M.A.N._Madrid)_03.jpg"><img alt="Bas-relief of Jupiter, nude from the waist up and seated on a throne" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1825" data-file-width="1119" decoding="async" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Puteal_de_la_Moncloa_%28M.A.N._Madrid%29_03.jpg/170px-Puteal_de_la_Moncloa_%28M.A.N._Madrid%29_03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Puteal_de_la_Moncloa_%28M.A.N._Madrid%29_03.jpg/255px-Puteal_de_la_Moncloa_%28M.A.N._Madrid%29_03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Puteal_de_la_Moncloa_%28M.A.N._Madrid%29_03.jpg/340px-Puteal_de_la_Moncloa_%28M.A.N._Madrid%29_03.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Puteal_de_la_Moncloa_(M.A.N._Madrid)_03.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Neo-Attic" title="Neo-Attic">Neo-Attic</a> bas-relief sculpture of Jupiter, holding a thunderbolt in his right hand; detail from the <i>Moncloa <a href="/wiki/Puteal" title="Puteal">Puteal</a></i> (Roman, 2nd century), National Archaeological Museum, Madrid</div></div></div>
<p>Older forms of the deity's name in Rome were <i>Dieus-pater</i> ("day/sky-father"), then <i>Diéspiter</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup> The 19th-century philologist <a href="/wiki/Georg_Wissowa" title="Georg Wissowa">Georg Wissowa</a> asserted these names are conceptually- and linguistically-connected to <i>Diovis</i> and <i>Diovis Pater</i>; he compares the analogous formations <i>Vedius</i>-<i>Veiove</i> and <i>fulgur Dium</i>, as opposed to <i>fulgur Summanum</i> (nocturnal lightning bolt) and <i>flamen Dialis</i> (based on <i>Dius</i>, <i>dies</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWissowa1912100_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWissowa1912100-109">[108]</a></sup> The Ancient later viewed them as entities separate from Jupiter. The terms are similar in etymology and semantics (<i>dies</i>, "daylight" and <i>Dius</i>, "daytime sky"), but differ linguistically. Wissowa considers the epithet <i>Dianus</i> noteworthy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> <i>Dieus</i> is the etymological equivalent of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a></i> and of the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Teutonics'</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ziu" title="Ziu">Ziu</a></i> (genitive <i>Ziewes</i>). The Indo-European deity is the god from which the names and partially the theology of Jupiter, Zeus and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Aryans" title="Indo-Aryans">Indo-Aryan</a> <a href="/wiki/Vedic_mythology" title="Vedic mythology">Vedic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dyaus_Pita" title="Dyaus Pita">Dyaus Pita</a> derive or have developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Roman practice of swearing by Jove to witness an oath in law courts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup> is the origin of the expression "by Jove!"—archaic, but still in use. The name of the god was also adopted as the name of the planet <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">adjective</a> "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/jovial" title="wikt:jovial">jovial</a>" originally described those born under the planet of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup> (reputed to be jolly, optimistic, and buoyant in <a href="/wiki/Temperament" title="Temperament">temperament</a>).
</p><p>Jove was the original namesake of Latin forms of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Week-day_names" title="Week-day names">weekday</a> now known in English as <a href="/wiki/Thursday#Origins_of_the_name" title="Thursday">Thursday</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> (originally called <i>Iovis Dies</i> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>). These became <i>jeudi</i> in <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>, <i>jueves</i> in <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>, <i>joi</i> in <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">Romanian</a>, <i>giovedì</i> in <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>, <i>dijous</i> in <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">Catalan</a>, <i>Xoves</i> in <a href="/wiki/Galician_language" title="Galician language">Galician</a>, <i>Joibe</i> in <a href="/wiki/Friulian_language" title="Friulian language">Friulian</a>, <i>Dijóu</i> in <a href="/wiki/Franco-Proven%C3%A7al_language" title="Franco-Provençal language">Provençal</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_epithets">Major epithets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Major epithets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Epithets_of_Jupiter" title="Epithets of Jupiter">Epithets of Jupiter</a></div>
<p>The epithets of a Roman god indicate his theological qualities. The study of these epithets must consider their origins (the historical context of an epithet's source).
</p><p>Jupiter's most ancient attested forms of cult belong to the State cult: these include the mount cult (see section above note n. 22).  In Rome this cult entailed the existence of particular sanctuaries the most important of which were located on <i>Mons Capitolinus</i> (earlier <i>Tarpeius</i>). The mount had two tops that were both destined to the discharge of acts of cult related to Jupiter. The northern and higher top was the <i><a href="/wiki/Arx_(Roman)" title="Arx (Roman)">arx</a></i> and on it was located the observation place of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augurs" title="Augurs">augurs</a> <i>(<a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_ancient_Roman_religion#auguraculum" title="Glossary of ancient Roman religion">auguraculum</a>)</i> and to it headed the monthly procession of the <i>sacra Idulia</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup> On the southern top was to be found the most ancient sanctuary of the god: the shrine of  <i>Iuppiter Feretrius</i> allegedly built by Romulus, restored by Augustus. The god here had no image and was represented by the sacred flintstone (<i>silex</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> The most ancient known rites, those of the <i>spolia opima</i> and of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fetials" title="Fetials">fetials</a> which connect Jupiter with Mars and Quirinus are dedicated to <i>Iuppiter Feretrius</i> or <i>Iuppiter Lapis</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup> The concept of the sky god was already overlapped with the ethical and political domain since this early time. According to Wissowa and Dumézil<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G._Dumézil_ARR_above_p._169_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-G._Dumézil_ARR_above_p._169-119">[118]</a></sup> <i>Iuppiter Lapis</i> seems to be inseparable from <i>Iuppiter Feretrius</i> in whose tiny templet on the Capitol the stone was lodged.
</p><p>Another most ancient epithet is <i>Lucetius</i>: although the Ancients, followed by some modern scholars such as Wissowa,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWissowa1912100_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWissowa1912100-109">[108]</a></sup> interpreted it as referring to sunlight, the <i>carmen Saliare</i> shows that it refers to lightning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup> A further confirmation of this interpretation is provided by the sacred meaning of lightning which is reflected in the sensitivity of the <i>flaminica Dialis</i> to the phenomenon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup> To the same atmospheric complex belongs the epithet <i>Elicius</i>: while the ancient erudites thought it was connected to lightning, it is in fact related to the opening of the rervoirs of rain, as is testified by the ceremony of the <i>Nudipedalia</i>, meant to propitiate rainfall and devoted to Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup> and the ritual of the <i><a href="/wiki/Lapis_manalis" title="Lapis manalis">lapis manalis</a></i>, the stone which was brought into the city through the <i>Porta Capena</i> and carried around in times of drought, which was named <i>Aquaelicium</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup> Other early epithets connected with the atmospheric quality of Jupiter are <i>Pluvius</i>, <i>Imbricius</i>, <i>Tempestas</i>, <i>Tonitrualis</i>, <i>tempestatium divinarum potens</i>, <i>Serenator</i>, <i>Serenus</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup> and, referred to lightning, <i>Fulgur</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup> <i>Fulgur Fulmen</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup> later as nomen agentis  <i>Fulgurator</i>, <i>Fulminator</i>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup> the high antiquity of the cult is testified by the neutre form <i>Fulgur</i> and the use of the term for the <i>bidental</i>, the lightning well dug on the spot hit by a lightning bolt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
