<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_bronze_Luxembourg_MNHA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1719" data-file-width="933" decoding="async" height="405" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Jupiter_bronze_Luxembourg_MNHA.jpg/220px-Jupiter_bronze_Luxembourg_MNHA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Jupiter_bronze_Luxembourg_MNHA.jpg/330px-Jupiter_bronze_Luxembourg_MNHA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Jupiter_bronze_Luxembourg_MNHA.jpg/440px-Jupiter_bronze_Luxembourg_MNHA.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_bronze_Luxembourg_MNHA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A bronze statue of Jupiter, from the territory of the <a href="/wiki/Treveri" title="Treveri">Treveri</a></div></div></div>
<p>A group of epithets has been interpreted by Wissowa (and his followers) as a reflection of the agricultural or warring nature of the god, some of which are also in the list of eleven preserved by Augustine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup> The agricultural ones include <i>Opitulus</i>, <i>Almus</i>, <i>Ruminus</i>, <i>Frugifer</i>, <i>Farreus</i>, <i>Pecunia</i>, <i>Dapalis</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup> <i>Epulo</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup> Augustine gives an explanation of the ones he lists which should reflect Varro's: <i>Opitulus</i> because he brings <i>opem</i> (means, relief) to the needy, <i>Almus</i> because he nourishes everything, <i>Ruminus</i> because he nourishes the living beings by breastfeeding them, <i>Pecunia</i> because everything belongs to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup>
Dumézil maintains the cult usage of these epithets is not documented and that the epithet Ruminus, as Wissowa and Latte remarked, may not have the meaning given by Augustine but it should be understood as part of a series including  <i>Rumina</i>, <i> Ruminalis ficus</i>, <i>Iuppiter Ruminus</i>, which bears the name of Rome itself with an Etruscan vocalism preserved in inscriptions, series that would be preserved in the sacred language (cf. <i>Rumach</i> Etruscan for Roman). However many scholars have argued that the name of Rome, <i>Ruma</i>, meant in fact woman's breast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diva_Rumina" title="Diva Rumina">Diva Rumina</a>, as Augustine testifies in the cited passage, was the goddess of suckling babies: she was venerated near the <i>ficus ruminalis</i> and was offered only libations of milk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[135]</a></sup> Here moreover Augustine cites the verses devoted to Jupiter by <a href="/wiki/Quintus_Valerius_Soranus" title="Quintus Valerius Soranus">Quintus Valerius Soranus</a>, while hypothesising <i>Iuno</i> (more adept in his view as a breastfeeder), i.e. Rumina instead of Ruminus, might be nothing else than  <i>Iuppiter</i>: "<i>Iuppiter omnipotens regum rerumque deumque Progenitor genetrixque deum...</i>".
</p><p>In Dumézil's opinion <i>Farreus</i> should be understood as related to the rite of the <i>confarreatio</i> the most sacred form of marriage, the name of which is due to the spelt cake eaten by the spouses, rather than surmising an agricultural quality of the god: the epithet means the god was the guarantor of the effects of the ceremony, to which the presence of his flamen is necessary and that he can interrupt with a clap of thunder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>The epithet <i>Dapalis</i> is on the other hand connected to a rite described by Cato and mentioned by Festus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup> Before the sowing of autumn or spring the peasant offered a banquet of roast beef and a cup of wine to Jupiter : it is natural that on such occasions he would entreat the god who has power over the weather, however Cato' s prayer of s one of sheer offer and no request. The language suggests another attitude: Jupiter is invited to a banquet which is supposedly abundant and magnificent. The god is honoured as <i>summus</i>. The peasant may hope he shall receive a benefit, but he does not say it. This interpretation finds support in the analogous urban ceremony of the <i>epulum Iovis</i>, from which the god derives the epithet of  <i>Epulo</i>  and which was a magnificent feast accompanied by flutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>Epithets related to warring are in Wissowa's view  <i>Iuppiter Feretrius</i>, <i>Iuppiter Stator</i>, <i>Iuppiter Victor</i> and <i>Iuppiter Invictus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWissowa1912105-108_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWissowa1912105-108-140">[139]</a></sup> <i>Feretrius</i> would be connected with war by the rite of the first type of  <i><a href="/wiki/Spolia_opima" title="Spolia opima">spolia opima</a></i> which is in fact a dedication to the god of the arms of the defeated king of the enemy that happens whenever he has been killed by the king of Rome or his equivalent authority. Here too Dumézil notes the dedication has to do with regality and not with war, since the rite is in fact the offer of the arms of a king by a king:  a proof of such an assumption is provided by the fact that the arms of an enemy king captured by an officer or a common soldier were dedicated to Mars and Quirinus respectively.
</p><p><i>Iuppiter Stator</i> was first attributed by tradition to <a href="/wiki/Romulus" title="Romulus">Romulus</a>, who had prayed the god for his almighty help at a difficult time the battle with the Sabines of king Titus Tatius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[140]</a></sup> Dumézil opines the action of Jupiter is not that of a god of war who wins through fighting: Jupiter acts by causing an inexplicable change in the morale of the fighters of the two sides. The same feature can be detected also in the certainly historical record of the battle of the third Samnite War in 294 BC, in which consul <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Atilius_Regulus" title="Marcus Atilius Regulus">Marcus Atilius Regulus</a> vowed a temple to <i>Iuppiter Stator</i> if "Jupiter will stop the rout of the Roman army and if afterwards the Samnite legions shall be victouriously massacred...It looked as if the gods themselves had taken side with Romans, so much easily did the Roman arms succeed in prevailing...".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[142]</a></sup> In a similar manner one can explain the epithet <i>Victor</i>, whose cult was founded in 295 BC on the battlefield of <a href="/wiki/Sentinum" title="Sentinum">Sentinum</a> by <a href="/wiki/Quintus_Fabius_Maximus_Gurges_(consul_292_BC)" title="Quintus Fabius Maximus Gurges (consul 292 BC)">Quintus Fabius Maximus Gurges</a> and who received another vow again in 293 by consul <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Papirius_Cursor" title="Lucius Papirius Cursor">Lucius Papirius Cursor</a> before a battle against the Samnite <i>legio linteata</i>. The religious meaning of the vow is in both cases an appeal to the supreme god by a Roman chief at a time of need for divine help from the supreme god, albeit for different reasons: Fabius had remained the only political and military responsible of the Roman State after the <i>devotio</i> of P. Decius Mus, Papirius had to face an enemy who had acted with impious rites and vows, i.e. was religiously reprehensible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>More recently Dario Sabbatucci has given a different interpretation of the meaning of <i>Stator</i> within the frame of his structuralistic and dialectic vision of Roman calendar, identifying oppositions, tensions and equilibria: January is the month of <a href="/wiki/Janus" title="Janus">Janus</a>, at the beginning of the year, in the uncertain time of winter (the most ancient calendar had only ten months, from March to December). In this month Janus deifies kingship and defies Jupiter. Moreover, January sees also the presence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veiovis" title="Veiovis">Veiovis</a> who appears as an anti-Jupiter, of <a href="/wiki/Carmenta" title="Carmenta">Carmenta</a> who is the goddess of birth and like Janus has two opposed faces, <i>Prorsa</i> and <i>Postvorta</i> (also named <a href="/wiki/Antevorta" title="Antevorta">Antevorta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antevorta" title="Antevorta">Porrima</a>), of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iuturna" title="Iuturna">Iuturna</a>, who as a gushing spring evokes the process of coming into being from non-being as the god of passage and change does. In this period the preeminence of Janus needs compensating on the Ides through the action of Jupiter <i>Stator</i>, who plays the role of anti-Janus, i.e. of moderator of the action of Janus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Epithets_denoting_functionality">Epithets denoting functionality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Epithets denoting functionality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some epithets describe a particular aspect of the god, or one of his functions:
</p>
<ul><li><b>Jove Aegiochus</b>, Jove "Holder of the Goat or Aegis", as the father of <a href="/wiki/Aegipan" title="Aegipan">Aegipan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[145]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Jupiter Caelus</b>, Jupiter as the sky or heavens; see also <a href="/wiki/Caelus" title="Caelus">Caelus</a>.</li>
<li><b>Jupiter Caelestis</b>, "Heavenly" or "Celestial Jupiter".</li>
<li><b>Jupiter Elicius</b>, Jupiter "who calls forth [celestial omens]" or "who is called forth [by incantations]"; "sender of rain".</li>
<li><b>Jupiter Feretrius</b>, who carries away the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_looting" title="War looting">spoils of war</a>". Feretrius was called upon to witness solemn oaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[146]</a></sup> The epithet or "<a href="/wiki/Numen" title="Numen">numen</a>" is probably connected with the verb <i>ferire</i>, "to strike," referring to a ritual striking of ritual as illustrated in <i>foedus ferire</i>, of which the <i>silex</i>, a quartz rock, is evidence in his temple on the Capitoline hill, which is said to have been the first temple in Rome, erected and dedicated by <a href="/wiki/Romulus" title="Romulus">Romulus</a> to commemorate his winning of the <i>spolia opima</i> from Acron, king of the Caeninenses, and to serve as a repository for them. <i>Iuppiter Feretrius</i> was therefore equivalent to <i>Iuppiter Lapis</i>, the latter used for a specially solemn oath.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[147]</a></sup> According to Livy I 10, 5 and Plutarch <i>Marcellus</i> 8 though, the meaning of this epithet is related to the peculiar frame used to carry the <i>spolia opima</i> to the god, the <i>feretrum</i>, itself from verb <i>fero</i>,</li>
<li><b>Jupiter Centumpeda</b>, literally, "he who has one hundred feet"; that is, "he who has the power of establishing, of rendering stable, bestowing stability on everything", since he himself is the paramount of stability.</li>
<li><b>Jupiter Fulgur</b> ("Lightning Jupiter"), <b>Fulgurator</b> or <b>Fulgens</b></li>
<li><b>Jupiter Lucetius</b> ("of the light"), an epithet almost certainly related to the light or flame of lightningbolts and not to daylight, as indicated by the Jovian verses of the <a href="/wiki/Carmen_Saliare" title="Carmen Saliare">carmen Saliare</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[148]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jupiter_Optimus_Maximus" title="Jupiter Optimus Maximus">Jupiter Optimus Maximus</a></b> (" the best and greatest"). <i>Optumus</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[149]</a></sup> because of the benefits he bestows, <i>Maximus</i> because of his strength, according to Cicero <i>Pro Domo Sua</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[150]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pluvius" title="Pluvius">Jupiter Pluvius</a></b>, "sender of rain".</li>
<li><b>Jupiter Ruminus</b>, "breastfeeder of every living being", according to Augustine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[151]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Jupiter Stator</b>, from <i>stare</i>, "to stand": "he who has power of founding, instituting everything", thence also he who bestows the power of resistance, making people, soldiers, stand firm and fast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[152]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jupiter_Summanus" title="Jupiter Summanus">Jupiter Summanus</a></b>, sender of nocturnal thunder</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terminus_(mythology)" title="Terminus (mythology)">Jupiter Terminalus</a></b> or <b>Iuppiter Terminus</b>, patron and defender of boundaries</li>
<li><b>Jupiter Tigillus</b>, "beam or shaft that supports and holds together the universe."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Augustine_CD_VII_11_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Augustine_CD_VII_11-154">[153]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Jupiter_Tonans" title="Jupiter Tonans">Jupiter Tonans</a></b>, "thunderer"</li>
<li><b>Jupiter Victor</b>, "he who has the power of conquering everything."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Augustine_CD_VII_11_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-Augustine_CD_VII_11-154">[153]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Syncretic_or_geographical_epithets">Syncretic or geographical epithets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Syncretic or geographical epithets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some epithets of Jupiter indicate his association with a particular place. Epithets found in the provinces of the Roman Empire may identify Jupiter with a local deity or site (see <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretism</a>).
</p>
<ul><li><b>Jupiter Ammon</b>, Jupiter equated with the Egyptian deity <a href="/wiki/Amun" title="Amun">Amun</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Egypt_(Roman_province)" title="Egypt (Roman province)">Roman conquest of Egypt</a></li>
<li><b>Jupiter Brixianus</b>, Jupiter equated with the local god of the town of <a href="/wiki/Brescia" title="Brescia">Brescia</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cisalpine_Gaul" title="Cisalpine Gaul">Cisalpine Gaul</a> (modern North <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>)</li>
<li><b>Jupiter Capitolinus</b>, also Jupiter Optimus Maximus, venerated throughout the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> at sites with a <i>Capitol</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capitolium" title="Capitolium">Capitolium</a>)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Jupiter_Dolichenus" title="Jupiter Dolichenus">Jupiter Dolichenus</a></b>, from <a href="/wiki/Gaziantep" title="Gaziantep">Doliche</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syria_(Roman_province)" title="Syria (Roman province)">Syria</a>, originally a <a href="/wiki/Baal" title="Baal">Baal</a> weather and war god. From the time of <a href="/wiki/Vespasian" title="Vespasian">Vespasian</a>, he was popular among the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_legions" title="Roman legions">Roman legions</a> as god of war and victory, especially on the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a> at <a href="/wiki/Carnuntum" title="Carnuntum">Carnuntum</a>. He is depicted as standing on a bull, with a thunderbolt in his left hand, and a double ax in the right.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Jupiter_Indiges" title="Jupiter Indiges">Jupiter Indiges</a></b>, "Jupiter of the country," a title given to <a href="/wiki/Aeneas" title="Aeneas">Aeneas</a> after his death, according to <a href="/wiki/Livy" title="Livy">Livy</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[154]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Jupiter Ladicus</b>, Jupiter equated with a Celtiberian mountain-god and worshipped as the spirit of Mount Ladicus in <a href="/wiki/Gallaecia" title="Gallaecia">Gallaecia</a>, northwest Iberia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[155]</a></sup> preserved in the toponym <i>Codos de Ladoco</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[156]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Jupiter Laterius</b> or <b>Latiaris</b>, the god of <a href="/wiki/Latium" title="Latium">Latium</a></li>
<li><b>Jupiter Parthinus</b> or <b>Partinus</b>, under this name was worshiped on the borders of northeast <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia_(Roman_province)" title="Dalmatia (Roman province)">Dalmatia</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_Moesia" title="Upper Moesia">Upper Moesia</a>, perhaps associated with the local tribe known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Partheni" title="Partheni">Partheni</a>.</li>
<li><b>Jupiter Poeninus</b>, under this name worshipped in the Alps, around the <a href="/wiki/Great_St_Bernard_Pass" title="Great St Bernard Pass">Great St Bernard Pass</a>, where he had a sanctuary.</li>
<li><b>Jupiter Solutorius</b>, a local version of Jupiter worshipped in <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>; he was syncretised with the local <a href="/wiki/Iberians" title="Iberians">Iberian</a> god <a href="/wiki/Eacus_(god)" title="Eacus (god)">Eacus</a>.</li>
<li><b>Jupiter Taranis</b>, Jupiter equated with the Celtic god <a href="/wiki/Taranis" title="Taranis">Taranis</a>.</li>
<li><b>Jupiter Uxellinus</b>, Jupiter as a god of high mountains.</li></ul>
<p>In addition, many of the epithets of Zeus can be found applied to Jupiter, by <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interpretatio_romana" title="Interpretatio romana">interpretatio romana</a></i>. Thus, since the hero <a href="/wiki/Trophonius" title="Trophonius">Trophonius</a> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lebadea" title="Lebadea">Lebadea</a> in Boeotia) is called Zeus Trophonius, this can be represented in English (as it would be in Latin) as Jupiter Trophonius. Similarly, the Greek cult of Zeus <a href="/wiki/Meilichios" title="Meilichios">Meilichios</a> appears in Pompeii as Jupiter Meilichius. Except in representing actual cults in Italy, this is largely 19th-century usage; modern works distinguish Jupiter from Zeus.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theology">Theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Theology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Marcus_Terentius_Varro" title="Marcus Terentius Varro">Marcus Terentius Varro</a> and <a href="/wiki/Verrius_Flaccus" title="Verrius Flaccus">Verrius Flaccus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[157]</a></sup> were the main sources on the theology of Jupiter and archaic Roman religion in general. Varro was acquainted with the <i><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_ancient_Roman_religion#libri_pontificales" title="Glossary of ancient Roman religion">libri pontificum</a></i> ("books of the <a href="/wiki/College_of_Pontiffs" title="College of Pontiffs">Pontiffs</a>") and their archaic classifications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[158]</a></sup> On these two sources depend other ancient authorities, such as <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Servius" title="Servius">Servius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aulus_Gellius" title="Aulus Gellius">Aulus Gellius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macrobius" title="Macrobius">Macrobius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Church_Fathers" title="Church Fathers">patristic texts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dionysius_of_Halicarnassus" title="Dionysius of Halicarnassus">Dionysius of Halicarnassus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a>.
</p><p>One of the most important sources which preserve the theology of Jupiter and other <a href="/wiki/List_of_Roman_deities" title="List of Roman deities">Roman deities</a> is <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_of_God_(book)" title="City of God (book)">The City of God against the Pagans</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a>. Augustine's criticism of traditional Roman religion is based on Varro's lost work, <i><a href="/wiki/Res_divina" title="Res divina">Antiquitates Rerum Divinarum</a></i>. Although a work of <a href="/wiki/Christian_apologetics" title="Christian apologetics">Christian apologetics</a>, <i>The City of God</i> provides glimpses into Varro's theological system and authentic Roman theological lore in general. According to Augustine,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup> Varro drew on the pontiff <a href="/wiki/Quintus_Mucius_Scaevola_Pontifex" title="Quintus Mucius Scaevola Pontifex">Mucius Scaevola</a>'s tripartite theology:
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Mythical_theology" title="Mythical theology">mythic theology</a> of the poets (useful for the <a href="/wiki/Theatre_of_ancient_Rome" title="Theatre of ancient Rome">theatre</a>)</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Natural_theology" title="Natural theology">physical theology</a> of the philosophers (useful for understanding the natural world)</li>
<li>The civil theology of the priests (useful for the state)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[160]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jovian_theology"><span id="Theology_of_Jupiter_in_early_Rome"></span>Jovian theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Jovian theology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Georg_Wissowa" title="Georg Wissowa">Georg Wissowa</a> stressed Jupiter's uniqueness as the only case among Indo-European religions in which the original god preserved his name, his identity and his prerogatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup> In this view, Jupiter is the god of heaven and retains his identification with the sky among the Latin poets (his name is used as a synonym for "sky".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup>) In this respect, he differs from his Greek equivalent Zeus (who is considered a personal god, warden and dispenser of skylight). His name reflects this idea; it is a derivative of the Indo-European word for "bright, shining sky". His residence is found atop the hills of Rome and of mountains in general; as a result, his cult is present in Rome and throughout Italy at upper elevations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[163]</a></sup> Jupiter assumed atmospheric qualities; he is the wielder of lightning and the master of weather. However, Wissowa acknowledges that Jupiter is not merely a naturalistic, heavenly, supreme deity; he is in continual communication with man by means of thunder, lightning and the flight of birds (his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auspices" title="Auspices">auspices</a>). Through his vigilant watch he is also the guardian of public oaths and compacts and the guarantor of good faith in the State cult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[164]</a></sup> The Jovian cult was common to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italic_people" title="Italic people">Italic people</a> under the names <i>Iove</i>, <i>Diove</i> (Latin) and  <i>Iuve</i>, <i>Diuve</i> (Oscan, in Umbrian only <i>Iuve</i>, <i>Iupater</i> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iguvine_Tables" title="Iguvine Tables">Iguvine Tables</a>).
</p><p>Wissowa considered Jupiter also a god of war and agriculture, in addition to his political role as guarantor of good faith (public and private) as <i>Iuppiter Lapis</i> and <i>Dius Fidius</i>, respectively. His view is grounded in the sphere of action of the god (who intervenes in battle and influences the harvest through weather).<a href="#CITEREFWissowa1912">Wissowa (1912)</a>, pp. 103–108
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Georges_Dum%C3%A9zil" title="Georges Dumézil">Georges Dumézil</a>'s view, Jovian theology (and that of the equivalent gods in other Indo-European religions) is an evolution from a naturalistic, supreme, celestial god identified with heaven to a sovereign god, a wielder of lightning bolts, master and protector of the community (in other words, of a change from a naturalistic approach to the world of the divine to a socio-political approach).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giorgione,_The_tempest.jpg"><img alt="Painting of a mother feeding her child, watched by a shepherd, with lightning flashing across a dark sky in the background" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3666" data-file-width="3285" decoding="async" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Giorgione%2C_The_tempest.jpg/220px-Giorgione%2C_The_tempest.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Giorgione%2C_The_tempest.jpg/330px-Giorgione%2C_The_tempest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Giorgione%2C_The_tempest.jpg/440px-Giorgione%2C_The_tempest.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Giorgione,_The_tempest.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One interpretation of the lightning in <a href="/wiki/Giorgione" title="Giorgione">Giorgione</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tempest_(Giorgione)" title="The Tempest (Giorgione)">Tempest</a></i> is that it represents the presence of Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[166]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Vedic_mythology" title="Vedic mythology">Vedic religion</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dyaus_Pitar" title="Dyaus Pitar">Dyaus Pitar</a> remained confined to his distant, removed, passive role and the place of sovereign god was occupied by <a href="/wiki/Varuna" title="Varuna">Varuna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mitra" title="Mitra">Mitra</a>. In Greek and Roman religion, instead, the homonymous gods <i>*Diou-</i> and <i>Δι<a href="/wiki/Digamma" title="Digamma">ϝ</a>-</i> evolved into atmospheric deities; by their mastery of thunder and lightning, they expressed themselves and made their will known to the community. In Rome, Jupiter also sent signs to the leaders of the state in the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auspices" title="Auspices">auspices</a> in addition to thunder. The art of <a href="/wiki/Augury" title="Augury">augury</a> was considered prestigious by ancient Romans; by sending his signs, Jupiter (the sovereign of heaven) communicates his advice to his terrestrial colleague: the king (<i>rex</i>) or his successor magistrates. The encounter between the heavenly and political, legal aspects of the deity are well represented by the prerogatives, privileges, functions and taboos proper to his <i><a href="/wiki/Flamen" title="Flamen">flamen</a></i> (the <i><a href="/wiki/Flamen_Dialis" title="Flamen Dialis">flamen Dialis</a></i> and his wife, the <i>flaminica Dialis</i>).
</p><p>Dumézil maintains that Jupiter is not himself a god of war and agriculture, although his actions and interest may extend to these spheres of human endeavour. His view is based on the methodological assumption that the chief criterion for studying a god's nature is not to consider his field of action, but the quality, method and features of his action. Consequently, the analysis of the type of action performed by Jupiter in the domains in which he operates indicates that Jupiter is a sovereign god who may act in the field of politics (as well as agriculture and war) in his capacity as such, i.e. in a way and with the features proper to a king. Sovereignty is expressed through the two aspects of absolute, magic power (epitomised and represented by the Vedic god <a href="/wiki/Varuna" title="Varuna">Varuna</a>) and lawful right (by the Vedic god <a href="/wiki/Mitra" title="Mitra">Mitra</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[167]</a></sup> However, sovereignty permits action in every field; otherwise, it would lose its essential quality. As a further proof, Dumézil cites the story of Tullus Hostilius (the most belligerent of the Roman kings), who was killed by Jupiter with a lightning bolt (indicating that he did not enjoy the god's favour). 
Varro's definition of Jupiter as the god who has under his jurisdiction the full expression of every being (<i>penes Iovem sunt summa</i>) reflects the sovereign nature of the god, as opposed to the jurisdiction of Janus (god of passages and change) on their beginning (<i>penes Ianum sunt prima</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[168]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_to_other_gods">Relation to other gods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Relation to other gods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Capitoline_Triad">Capitoline Triad</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Capitoline Triad">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Capitoline_Triad" title="Capitoline Triad">Capitoline Triad</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arte_romana,_triade_capitolina,_160-180_dc_(guidonia_montecelio,_museo_civico_archeologico)_01.jpg"><img alt="Statue of three figures, seated side by side" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2940" data-file-width="4014" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Arte_romana%2C_triade_capitolina%2C_160-180_dc_%28guidonia_montecelio%2C_museo_civico_archeologico%29_01.jpg/220px-Arte_romana%2C_triade_capitolina%2C_160-180_dc_%28guidonia_montecelio%2C_museo_civico_archeologico%29_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Arte_romana%2C_triade_capitolina%2C_160-180_dc_%28guidonia_montecelio%2C_museo_civico_archeologico%29_01.jpg/330px-Arte_romana%2C_triade_capitolina%2C_160-180_dc_%28guidonia_montecelio%2C_museo_civico_archeologico%29_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Arte_romana%2C_triade_capitolina%2C_160-180_dc_%28guidonia_montecelio%2C_museo_civico_archeologico%29_01.jpg/440px-Arte_romana%2C_triade_capitolina%2C_160-180_dc_%28guidonia_montecelio%2C_museo_civico_archeologico%29_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arte_romana,_triade_capitolina,_160-180_dc_(guidonia_montecelio,_museo_civico_archeologico)_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Capitoline Triad</div></div></div>
<p>The Capitoline Triad was introduced to Rome by the Tarquins. Dumézil<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[169]</a></sup> thinks it might have been an Etruscan (or local) creation based on Vitruvius' treatise on architecture, in which the three deities are associated as the most important. It is possible that the Etruscans paid particular attention to <a href="/wiki/Menrva" title="Menrva">Menrva</a> (Minerva) as a goddess of destiny, in addition to the royal couple Uni (Juno) and Tinia (Jupiter).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[170]</a></sup> In Rome, Minerva later assumed a military aspect under the influence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athena_Pallas" title="Athena Pallas">Athena Pallas</a> (Polias). Dumézil argues that with the advent of the Republic, Jupiter became the only king of Rome, no longer merely the first of the great gods.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaic_Triad">Archaic Triad</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Archaic Triad">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Archaic_Triad" title="Archaic Triad">Archaic Triad</a></div>
<p>The Archaic Triad is a hypothetical theological structure (or system) consisting of the gods Jupiter, Mars and Quirinus. It was first described by Wissowa,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[171]</a></sup> and the concept was developed further by Dumézil. The three-function hypothesis of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_religion" title="Proto-Indo-European religion">Indo-European society</a> advanced by Dumézil holds that in prehistory, society was divided into three classes (priests, warriors and craftsmen) which had as their religious counterparts the divine figures of the sovereign god, the warrior god and the civil god. The sovereign function (embodied by Jupiter) entailed omnipotence; thence, a domain extended over every aspect of nature and life. The colour relating to the sovereign function is white.
</p><p>The three functions are interrelated with one another, overlapping to some extent; the sovereign function, although essentially religious in nature, is involved in many ways in areas pertaining to the other two. Therefore, Jupiter is the "magic player" in the founding of the Roman state and the fields of war, agricultural plenty, human fertility and wealth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p>This hypothesis has not found widespread support among scholars.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jupiter_and_Minerva">Jupiter and Minerva</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Jupiter and Minerva">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Apart from being protectress of the arts and craft as Minerva Capta, who was brought from Falerii, Minerva's association to Jupiter and relevance to Roman state religion is mainly linked to the <a href="/wiki/Palladium_(classical_antiquity)" title="Palladium (classical antiquity)">Palladium</a>, a wooden statue of Athena that could move the eyes and wave the spear. It was stored in the <i>penus interior</i>, inner penus of the <i>aedes Vestae</i>, temple of Vesta and considered the most important among the <i><a href="/wiki/Pignora_imperii" title="Pignora imperii">pignora imperii</a></i>, pawns of dominion, empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[173]</a></sup> In Roman traditional lore it was brought from Troy by Aeneas.  Scholars though think it was last taken to Rome in the third or second century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[174]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Juno_and_Fortuna">Juno and Fortuna</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Juno and Fortuna">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The divine couple received from Greece its matrimonial implications, thence bestowing on <a href="/wiki/Juno_(mythology)" title="Juno (mythology)">Juno</a> the role of tutelary goddess of marriage (<i>Iuno Pronuba</i>).
</p><p>The couple itself though cannot be reduced to a Greek apport. The association of Juno and Jupiter is of the most ancient Latin theology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[175]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Praeneste" title="Praeneste">Praeneste</a> offers a glimpse into original Latin mythology: the local goddess <a href="/wiki/Fortuna" title="Fortuna">Fortuna</a> is represented as milking two infants, one male and one female, namely Jove (Jupiter) and Juno.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[176]</a></sup> It seems fairly safe to assume that from the earliest times they were identified by their own proper names and since they got them they were never changed through the course of history: they were called Jupiter and Juno. These gods were the most ancient deities of every Latin town. Praeneste preserved divine filiation and infancy as the sovereign god and his paredra Juno have a mother who is the primordial goddess Fortuna Primigenia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[177]</a></sup> Many terracotta statuettes have been discovered which represent a woman with a child: one of them represents exactly the scene described by Cicero of a woman with two children of different sex who touch her breast. Two of the votive inscriptions to Fortuna associate her and Jupiter: " Fortunae Iovi puero..." and "Fortunae Iovis puero..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[178]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1882 though R. Mowat published an inscription in which Fortuna is called <i>daughter of Jupiter</i>, raising new questions and opening new perspectives in the theology of Latin gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[179]</a></sup> Dumezil has elaborated an interpretative theory according to which this <i>aporia</i> would be an intrinsic, fundamental feature of Indoeuropean deities of the primordial and sovereign level, as it finds a parallel in Vedic religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[180]</a></sup> The contradiction would put Fortuna both at the origin of time and into its ensuing diachronic process: it is the comparison offered by Vedic deity <a href="/wiki/Aditi" title="Aditi">Aditi</a>, the <i>Not-Bound</i> or <i>Enemy of Bondage</i>, that shows that there is no question of choosing one of the two apparent options: as the mother of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aditya" title="Aditya">Aditya</a> she has the same type of relationship with one of his sons, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Dak%E1%B9%A3a&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dakṣa (page does not exist)">Dakṣa</a>, the minor sovereign. who represents the <i>Creative Energy</i>, being at the same time his mother and daughter, as is true for the whole group of sovereign gods to which she belongs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[181]</a></sup> Moreover, Aditi is thus one of the heirs (along with <a href="/wiki/Savitr" title="Savitr">Savitr</a>) of the opening god of the Indoiranians, as she is represented with her head on her two sides, with the two faces looking opposite directions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[182]</a></sup> The mother of the sovereign gods has thence two solidal but distinct modalities of duplicity, i.e. of having two foreheads and a double position in the genealogy. Angelo Brelich has interpreted this theology as the basic opposition between the primordial absence of order (chaos) and the organisation of the cosmos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[183]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Janus">Janus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Janus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Janus" title="Janus">Janus</a></div>
<p>The relation of Jupiter to Janus is problematic. Varro defines Jupiter as the god who has <i>potestas</i> (power) over the forces by which anything happens in the world. Janus, however, has the privilege of being invoked first in rites, since in his power are the beginnings of things (<i>prima</i>), the appearance of Jupiter included.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[184]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Saturn">Saturn</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Saturn">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Saturn_(mythology)" title="Saturn (mythology)">Saturn (mythology)</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latini" title="Latini">Latins</a> considered Saturn the predecessor of Jupiter. Saturn reigned in <a href="/wiki/Latium" title="Latium">Latium</a> during a mythical <a href="/wiki/Golden_Age" title="Golden Age">Golden Age</a> reenacted every year at the festival of <a href="/wiki/Saturnalia" title="Saturnalia">Saturnalia</a>. Saturn also retained primacy in matters of agriculture and money. Unlike the Greek tradition of <a href="/wiki/Cronus" title="Cronus">Cronus</a> and Zeus, the usurpation of Saturn as king of the gods by Jupiter was not viewed by the Latins as violent or hostile; Saturn continued to be revered in his temple at the foot of the Capitol Hill, which maintained the alternative name <i>Saturnius</i> into the time of Varro.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[185]</a></sup>
A. Pasqualini has argued that Saturn was related to <i>Iuppiter Latiaris</i>, the old Jupiter of the Latins, as the original figure of this Jupiter was superseded on the Alban Mount, whereas it preserved its gruesome character in the ceremony held at the sanctuary of the Latiar Hill in Rome which involved a human sacrifice and the aspersion of the statue of the god with the blood of the victim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[186]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fides">Fides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Fides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fides_(mythology)" title="Fides (mythology)">Fides (mythology)</a></div>
<p>The abstract <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Personification" title="Personification">personification</a> Fides ("Faith, Trust") was one of the oldest gods associated with Jupiter. As guarantor of public faith, Fides had her temple on the Capitol (near that of Capitoline Jupiter).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[187]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dius_Fidius">Dius Fidius</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Dius Fidius">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dius_Fidius" title="Dius Fidius">Dius Fidius</a></div>
<p><i>Dius Fidius</i> is considered a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theonym" title="Theonym">theonym</a> for Jupiter,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[188]</a></sup> and sometimes a separate entity also known in Rome as <a href="/wiki/Sancus" title="Sancus">Semo Sancus</a> Dius Fidius. Wissowa argued that while Jupiter is the god of the <i>Fides Publica Populi Romani</i> as <i>Iuppiter Lapis</i> (by whom important oaths are sworn), Dius Fidius is a deity established for everyday use and was charged with the protection of good faith in private affairs. Dius Fidius would thus correspond to <i>Zeus Pistios</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWissowa1912103_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWissowa1912103-190">[189]</a></sup> The association with Jupiter may be a matter of divine relation; some scholars see him as a form of Hercules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[190]</a></sup> Both Jupiter and Dius Fidius were wardens of oaths and wielders of lightning bolts; both required an opening in the roof of their temples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G._Dumézil_ARR_above_p._169_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-G._Dumézil_ARR_above_p._169-119">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>The functionality of Sancus occurs consistently within the sphere of <i>fides</i>, oaths and respect for contracts and of the divine-sanction guarantee against their breach. Wissowa suggested that Semo Sancus is the <i><a href="/wiki/Genius_(mythology)" title="Genius (mythology)">genius</a></i> of Jupiter,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roschers_Lexicon'_1912,_p._131_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roschers_Lexicon'_1912,_p._131-192">[191]</a></sup> but the concept of a deity's <i>genius</i> is a development of the Imperial period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[192]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some aspects of the oath-ritual for Dius Fidius (such as proceedings under the open sky or in the <i>compluvium</i> of private residences), and the fact the temple of Sancus had no roof, suggest that the oath sworn by Dius Fidius predated that for <i>Iuppiter Lapis</i> or <i>Iuppiter Feretrius</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[193]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genius">Genius</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Genius">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Genius_(mythology)" title="Genius (mythology)">Genius (mythology)</a></div>
<p>Augustine quotes Varro who explains the <i>genius</i> as "the god who is in charge and has the power to generate everything" and "the rational spirit of all (therefore, everyone has their own)". Augustine concludes that Jupiter should be considered the <i>genius</i> of the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[194]</a></sup>
</p><p>G. Wissowa advanced the hypothesis that Semo <a href="/wiki/Sancus" title="Sancus">Sancus</a> is the genius of Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roschers_Lexicon'_1912,_p._131_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roschers_Lexicon'_1912,_p._131-192">[191]</a></sup> W. W. Fowler has cautioned that this interpretation looks to be an anachronism and it would only be acceptable to say that Sancus is a <i>Genius Iovius</i>, as it appears from the Iguvine Tables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>Censorinus cites <a href="/wiki/Granius_Flaccus" title="Granius Flaccus">Granius Flaccus</a> as saying that "the Genius was the same entity as the Lar" in his lost work <i>De Indigitamentis</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[197]</a></sup> probably referring to the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lar_Familiaris" title="Lar Familiaris">Lar Familiaris</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Mutunus_Tutunus" title="Mutunus Tutunus">Mutunus Tutunus</a> had his shrine at the foot of the Velian Hill near those of the Di Penates and of Vica Pota, who were among the most ancient gods of the Roman community of according to Wissowa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[198]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dumézil opines that the attribution of a Genius to the gods should be earlier than its first attestation of 58 BC, in an inscription which mentions the <i>Iovis Genius</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[199]</a></sup>
</p><p>A connection between Genius and Jupiter seems apparent in <a href="/wiki/Plautus" title="Plautus">Plautus</a>' comedy <i><a href="/wiki/Amphitryon" title="Amphitryon">Amphitryon</a></i>, in which Jupiter takes up the looks of <a href="/wiki/Alcmene" title="Alcmene">Alcmena</a>'s husband in order to seduce her: J. Hubeaux sees there a reflection of the story that <a href="/wiki/Scipio_Africanus" title="Scipio Africanus">Scipio Africanus</a>' mother conceived him with a snake that was in fact Jupiter transformed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[200]</a></sup> Scipio himself claimed that only he would rise to the mansion of the gods through the widest gate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[201]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among the Etruscan <i>Penates</i> there is a <i>Genius Iovialis</i> who comes after <i>Fortuna</i> and <i>Ceres</i> and before <i>Pales</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[202]</a></sup>  Genius Iovialis is one of the <i>Penates</i> of the humans and not of Jupiter though, as these were located in region I of Martianus Capella' s division of Heaven, while Genius appears in regions V and VI along with Ceres, Favor (possibly a Roman approximation to an Etruscan male manifestation of Fortuna) and Pales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[203]</a></sup> This is in accord with the definition of the Penates of man being Fortuna, Ceres, Pales and Genius Iovialis and the statement in Macrobius that the Larentalia were dedicated to Jupiter as the god whence the souls of men come from and to whom they return after death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[204]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Summanus">Summanus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Summanus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Summanus" title="Summanus">Summanus</a></div>
<p>The god of nighttime lightning has been interpreted as an aspect of Jupiter, either a <a href="/wiki/Chthonic" title="Chthonic">chthonic</a> manifestation of the god or a separate god of the underworld. A statue of Summanus stood on the roof of the Temple of Capitoline Jupiter, and <i>Iuppiter Summanus</i> is one of the epithets of Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[205]</a></sup> Dumézil sees the opposition Dius Fidius versus Summanus as complementary, interpreting it as typical to the inherent ambiguity of the sovereign god exemplified by that of Mitra and Varuna in Vedic religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[206]</a></sup> The complementarity of the epithets is shown in inscriptions found on <i>puteal</i>s or <i>bidental</i>s reciting either <i>fulgur Dium conditum</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[207]</a></sup> or <i>fulgur Summanum conditum</i> in places struck by daytime versus nighttime lightning bolts respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[208]</a></sup> This is also consistent with the etymology of <i>Summanus</i>, deriving from <i>sub</i> and <i>mane</i> (the time before morning).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[209]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liber">Liber</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Liber">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Liber" title="Liber">Liber</a></div>
<p><i>Iuppiter</i> was associated with <a href="/wiki/Liber" title="Liber">Liber</a> through his epithet of <i>Liber</i> (association not yet been fully explained by scholars, due to the scarcity of early documentation).
In the past, it was maintained that Liber was only a progressively-detached <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/hypostasis" title="wikt:hypostasis">hypostasis</a> of Jupiter; consequently, the vintage festivals were to be attributed only to <i>Iuppiter Liber</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[210]</a></sup> Such a hypothesis was rejected as groundless by Wissowa, although he was a supporter of Liber's Jovian origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[211]</a></sup> Olivier de Cazanove<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[212]</a></sup>  contends that it is difficult to admit that Liber (who is present in the oldest calendars—those of Numa—in the <i>Liberalia</i> and in the month of <i>Liber</i> at Lavinium)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDVII21_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDVII21-214">[213]</a></sup> was derived from another deity. Such a derivation would find support only in epigraphic documents, primarily from the Osco-Sabellic area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[214]</a></sup> Wissowa sets the position of <i>Iuppiter Liber</i> within the framework of an agrarian Jupiter. The god also had a temple in this name on the Aventine in Rome, which was restored by Augustus and dedicated on September 1. Here, the god was sometimes named <i>Liber</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[215]</a></sup> and sometimes <i>Libertas</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[216]</a></sup> Wissowa opines that the relationship existed in the concept of creative abundance through which the supposedly-separate Liber might have been connected<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWissowa1912106_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWissowa1912106-218">[217]</a></sup> to the Greek god <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dionysos" title="Dionysos">Dionysos</a>, although both deities might not have been originally related to <a href="/wiki/Viticulture" title="Viticulture">viticulture</a>.
</p><p>Other scholars assert that there was no Liber (other than a god of wine) within historical memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[218]</a></sup> O. de Cazanove<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[219]</a></sup> argues that the domain of the sovereign god Jupiter was that of sacred, sacrificial wine (<i>vinum inferium</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[220]</a></sup> while that of Liber and Libera was confined to secular wine (<i>vinum spurcum</i>);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[221]</a></sup> these two types were obtained through differing fermentation processes. The offer of wine to Liber was made possible by naming the <i>mustum</i> (grape juice) stored in <a href="/wiki/Amphora" title="Amphora">amphoras</a> <i>sacrima</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[222]</a></sup>
Sacred wine was obtained by the natural fermentation of juice of grapes free from flaws of any type, religious (e. g. those struck by lightning, brought into contact with corpses or wounded people or coming from an unfertilised grapeyard) or secular (by "cutting" it with old wine). Secular (or "profane") wine was obtained through several types of manipulation (e.g.  by adding honey, or <i>mulsum</i>; using raisins, or <i>passum</i>; by boiling, or <i>defrutum</i>). However, the <i>sacrima</i> used for the offering to the two gods for the preservation of grapeyards, vessels and wine<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[223]</a></sup> was obtained only by pouring the juice into amphors after pressing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[224]</a></sup> The <i>mustum</i> was considered <i>spurcum</i> (dirty), and thus unusable in sacrifices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[225]</a></sup> The amphor (itself not an item of sacrifice) permitted presentation of its content on a table or could be added to a sacrifice; this happened at the <i>auspicatio vindamiae</i> for the first grape<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[226]</a></sup>  and for ears of corn of the <i>praemetium</i> on a dish (<i>lanx</i>) at the
temple of <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(mythology)" title="Ceres (mythology)">Ceres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[227]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dumézil, on the other hand, sees the relationship between Jupiter and Liber as grounded in the social and political relevance of the two gods (who were both considered patrons of freedom).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[228]</a></sup> The <i>Liberalia</i> of March were, since earliest times, the occasion for the ceremony of the donning of the <i>toga virilis</i> or <i>libera</i>  (which marked the passage into adult citizenship by young people). Augustine relates that these festivals had a particularly obscene character:  a <i>phallus</i> was taken to the fields on a cart, and then back in triumph to town. In <a href="/wiki/Lavinium" title="Lavinium">Lavinium</a> they lasted a month, during which the population enjoyed bawdy jokes. The most honest <i>matronae</i> were supposed to publicly crown the <i>phallus</i> with flowers, to ensure a good harvest and repeal the <i>fascinatio</i> (evil eye).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDVII21_214-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDVII21-214">[213]</a></sup> In Rome representations of the sex organs were placed in the temple of the couple <i>Liber Libera</i>, who presided over the male and female components of generation and the "liberation" of the semen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[229]</a></sup> This complex of rites and beliefs shows that the divine couple's jurisdiction extended over fertility in general, not only that of grapes. The etymology of  <i>Liber</i> (archaic form <i>Loifer, Loifir</i>) was explained by Émile Benveniste as formed on the IE theme *leudh- plus the suffix -es-; its original meaning is "the one of germination, he who ensures the sprouting of crops".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[230]</a></sup>
</p><p>The relationship of Jupiter with freedom was a common belief among the Roman people, as demonstrated by the dedication of the <i>Mons Sacer</i> to the god after the first secession of the <i>plebs</i>. Later inscriptions also show the unabated popular belief in Jupiter as bestower of freedom in the imperial era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[231]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Veiove">Veiove</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Veiove">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vejovis" title="Vejovis">Vejovis</a></div>
<p>Scholars have been often puzzled by Ve(d)iove (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veiovis" title="Veiovis">Veiovis</a>, or Vedius) and unwilling to discuss his identity, claiming our knowledge of this god is insufficient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[232]</a></sup> Most, however, agree that Veiove is a sort of special Jupiter or anti-Iove, or even an underworld Jupiter. In other words, Veiove is indeed the Capitoline god himself, who takes up a different, diminished appearance (<i>iuvenis</i> and <i>parvus</i>, young and gracile), in order to be able to discharge sovereign functions over places, times and spheres that by their own nature are excluded from the direct control of Jupiter as Optimus Maximus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[233]</a></sup> This conclusion is based on information provided by Gellius,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[234]</a></sup> who states his name is formed by adding prefix <i>ve</i> (here denoting "deprivation" or "negation") to <i>Iove </i> (whose name Gellius posits as rooted in the verb <i>iuvo</i> "I benefit"). D. Sabbatucci has stressed the feature of bearer of instability and antithesis to cosmic order of the god, who threatens the kingly power of Jupiter as <i>Stator</i> and <i>Centumpeda</i> and whose presence occurs side by side to Janus' on January 1, but also his function of helper to the growth of the young Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[235]</a></sup> In 1858 <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Preller" title="Ludwig Preller">Ludwig Preller</a> suggested that Veiovis may be the sinister double of Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[236]</a></sup>
</p><p>In fact, the god (under the name <i>Vetis</i>) is placed in the last case (number 16) of the outer rim of the Piacenza Liver—before <i>Cilens</i> (Nocturnus), who ends (or begins in the Etruscan vision) the disposition of the gods. In <a href="/wiki/Martianus_Capella" title="Martianus Capella">Martianus Capella</a>'s division of heaven, he is found in region XV with the <i>dii publici</i>; as such, he numbers among the infernal (or antipodal) gods. The location of his two temples in Rome—near those of Jupiter (one on the Capitoline Hill, in the low between the <i>arx</i> and the Capitolium, between the two groves where the <a href="/wiki/Asylum_(antiquity)" title="Asylum (antiquity)">asylum</a> founded by Romulus stood, the other on the Tiber Island near that of  <i>Iuppiter Iurarius</i>, later also known as temple of Aesculapius)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[237]</a></sup>—may be significant in this respect, along with the fact that he is considered the father<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[238]</a></sup> of Apollo, perhaps because he was depicted carrying arrows. He is also considered to be the unbearded Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[239]</a></sup> The dates of his festivals support the same conclusion: they fall on January 1,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[240]</a></sup> March 7<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[241]</a></sup> and May 21,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[242]</a></sup> the first date being the recurrence of the <a href="/wiki/Agonalia" title="Agonalia">Agonalia</a>, dedicated to Janus and celebrated by the king with the sacrifice of a ram. The nature of the sacrifice is debated; Gellius states <i>capra</i>, a female goat, although some scholars posit a ram. This sacrifice occurred <i>rito humano</i>, which may mean "with the rite appropriate for human sacrifice".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[243]</a></sup> Gellius concludes by stating that this god is one of those who receive sacrifices so as to persuade them to refrain from causing harm.
</p><p>The arrow is an ambivalent symbol; it was used in the ritual of the <a href="/wiki/Devotio" title="Devotio">devotio</a> (the general who vowed had to stand on an arrow).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[244]</a></sup> It is perhaps because of the arrow and of the juvenile looks that Gellius identifies Veiove with Apollo<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[245]</a></sup> and as a god who must receive worship in order to obtain his abstention from harming men, along with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robigus" title="Robigus">Robigus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Averruncus" title="Averruncus">Averruncus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[246]</a></sup> The ambivalence in the identity of Veiove is apparent in the fact that while he is present in places and times which may have a negative connotation (such as the <i>asylum</i> of Romulus in between the two groves on the Capitol, the Tiberine island along with Faunus and Aesculapius, the kalends of January, the nones of March, and May 21, a statue of his nonetheless stands in the <i>arx</i>. Moreover, the initial particle <i>ve-</i> which the ancient supposed were part of his name is itself ambivalent as it may have both an accrescitive and diminutive value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[247]</a></sup>
</p><p>Maurice Besnier has remarked that a temple to <i>Iuppiter</i> was dedicated by <i>praetor</i> Lucius Furius Purpureo before the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cremona_(200_BC)" title="Battle of Cremona (200 BC)">battle of Cremona</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Cenomani_(Cisalpine_Gaul)" title="Cenomani (Cisalpine Gaul)">Celtic Cenomani of Cisalpine Gaul</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[248]</a></sup> An inscription found at <a href="/wiki/Brescia" title="Brescia">Brescia</a> in 1888 shows that <i>Iuppiter Iurarius</i> was worshipped there<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[249]</a></sup> and one found on the south tip of Tiber Island in 1854 that there was a cult to the god on the spot too.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[250]</a></sup> Besnier speculates that Lucius Furius had evoked the chief god of the enemy and built a temple to him in Rome outside the <i>pomerium</i>. On January 1, the <i>Fasti Praenestini</i> record the festivals of Aesculapius and Vediove on the Island, while in the <i>Fasti</i> Ovid speaks of <i>Jupiter</i> and his grandson.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[251]</a></sup> Livy records that in 192 BC, <i>duumvir</i> Q. Marcus Ralla dedicated to Jupiter on the Capitol the two temples promised by L. Furius Purpureo, one of which was that promised during the war against the Gauls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[252]</a></sup> Besnier would accept a correction to Livy's passage (proposed by Jordan) to read <i>aedes Veiovi</i> instead of <i>aedes duae Iovi</i>.  Such a correction concerns the temples dedicated on the Capitol: it does not address the question of the dedication of the temple on the Island, which is puzzling, since the place is attested epigraphically as dedicated to the cult of  <i>Iuppiter Iurarius</i>, in the <i>Fasti Praenestini</i> of <i>Vediove</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[253]</a></sup> and to Jupiter according to Ovid. The two gods may have been seen as equivalent: <i>Iuppiter Iurarius</i> is an awesome and vengeful god, parallel to the Greek <i>Zeus Orkios</i>, the avenger of perjury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[254]</a></sup>
</p><p>A. Pasqualini has argued that Veiovis seems related to <i>Iuppiter Latiaris</i>, as the original figure of this Jupiter would have been superseded on the Alban Mount, whereas it preserved its gruesome character in the ceremony held on the sanctuary of the Latiar Hill, the southernmost hilltop of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quirinal" title="Quirinal">Quirinal</a> in Rome, which involved a human sacrifice. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gens_Iulia" title="Gens Iulia">gens Iulia</a> had gentilician cults at <a href="/wiki/Bovillae" title="Bovillae">Bovillae</a> where a dedicatory inscription to Vediove has been found in 1826 on an ara.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[255]</a></sup> According to Pasqualini it was a deity similar to Vediove, wielder of lightningbolts and chthonic, who was connected to the cult of the founders who first inhabited the Alban Mount and built the sanctuary. Such a cult once superseded on the Mount would have been taken up and preserved by the Iulii, private citizens bound to the <i>sacra Albana</i> by their Alban origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[256]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Victoria">Victoria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Victoria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Victoria_(mythology)" title="Victoria (mythology)">Victoria (mythology)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RSC_0009.jpg"><img alt="Roman coin, with bearded head on front and standing figure on reverse" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="250" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/RSC_0009.jpg/330px-RSC_0009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/RSC_0009.jpg/495px-RSC_0009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/11/RSC_0009.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RSC_0009.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coin with <a href="/wiki/Laurel_wreath" title="Laurel wreath">laureate</a> head of Jupiter (<a href="/wiki/Obverse_and_reverse" title="Obverse and reverse">obverse</a>) and (reverse) Victory, standing ("<i>ROMA</i>" below in <a href="/wiki/Relief" title="Relief">relief</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>Victoria was connected to <i>Iuppiter Victor</i> in his role as bestower of military victory. Jupiter, as a sovereign god, was considered as having the power to conquer anyone and anything in a supernatural way; his contribution to military victory was different from that of <a href="/wiki/Mars_(mythology)" title="Mars (mythology)">Mars</a> (god of military valour). Victoria appears first on the reverse of coins representing Venus (driving the quadriga of Jupiter, with her head crowned and with a palm in her hand) during the first Punic War.  Sometimes, she is represented walking and carrying a trophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[257]</a></sup>
</p><p>A temple was dedicated to the goddess afterwards on the Palatine, testifying to her high station in the Roman mind. When <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hieron_of_Syracuse" title="Hieron of Syracuse">Hieron of Syracuse</a> presented a golden statuette of the goddess to Rome, the Senate had it placed in the temple of Capitoline Jupiter among the greatest (and most sacred) deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[258]</a></sup>
Although Victoria played a significant role in the religious ideology of the late Republic and the Empire, she is undocumented in earlier times. A function similar to hers may have been played by the little-known <a href="/wiki/Vica_Pota" title="Vica Pota">Vica Pota</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminus">Terminus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Terminus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Terminus_(god)" title="Terminus (god)">Terminus (god)</a></div>
<p>Juventas and Terminus were the gods who, according to legend,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[259]</a></sup> refused to leave their sites on the Capitol when the construction of the temple of Jupiter was undertaken. Therefore, they had to be reserved a <i>sacellum</i> within the new temple. Their stubbornness was considered a good omen; it would guarantee youth, stability and safety to Rome on its site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[260]</a></sup> This legend is generally thought by scholars to indicate their strict connection with Jupiter. An inscription found near <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a> reads <i>Iuppiter Ter.</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[261]</a></sup> indicating that Terminus is an aspect of Jupiter.
</p><p>Terminus is the god of boundaries (public and private), as he is portrayed in literature. The religious value of the <a href="/wiki/Boundary_marker" title="Boundary marker">boundary marker</a> is documented by Plutarch,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[262]</a></sup> who ascribes to king Numa the construction of temples to Fides and Terminus and the delimitation of Roman territory. Ovid gives a vivid description of the rural rite at a boundary of fields of neighbouring peasants on February 23 (the day of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terminus_(mythology)#Worship" title="Terminus (mythology)">Terminalia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[263]</a></sup> On that day, Roman pontiffs and magistrates held a ceremony at the sixth mile of the <a href="/wiki/Via_Laurentina" title="Via Laurentina">Via Laurentina</a> (ancient border of the Roman <i>ager</i>, which maintained a religious value). 
This festival, however, marked the end of the year and was linked to time more directly than to space (as attested by Augustine's <a href="/wiki/Apologetics" title="Apologetics">apologia</a> on the role of Janus with respect to endings).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[264]</a></sup>  Dario Sabbatucci has emphasised the temporal affiliation of Terminus, a reminder of which is found in the rite of the <i>regifugium</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[265]</a></sup> 
G. Dumézil, on the other hand, views the function of this god as associated with the legalistic aspect of the sovereign function of Jupiter. Terminus would be the counterpart of the minor Vedic god Bagha, who oversees the just and fair division of goods among citizens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[266]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iuventas">Iuventas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Iuventas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iuventas" title="Iuventas">Iuventas</a></div>
<p>Along with <i>Terminus</i>, <i> Iuventas</i> (also known as  <i>Iuventus</i> and <i>Iuunta</i>) represents an aspect of Jupiter (as the legend of her refusal to leave the Capitol Hill demonstrates. Her name has the same root as <a href="/wiki/Juno_(mythology)" title="Juno (mythology)">Juno</a> (from <i>Iuu-</i>, "young, youngster"); the ceremonial litter bearing the sacred goose of Juno Moneta stopped before her <i>sacellum</i> on the festival of the goddess. Later, she was identified with the Greek <a href="/wiki/Hebe_(mythology)" title="Hebe (mythology)">Hebe</a>. The fact that Jupiter is related to the concept of youth is shown by his epithets <i>Puer</i>, <i>Iuuentus</i> and <i>Ioviste</i> (interpreted as "the youngest" by some scholars).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[267]</a></sup> Dumézil noted the presence of the two minor sovereign deities Bagha and <a href="/wiki/Aryaman" title="Aryaman">Aryaman</a> beside the Vedic sovereign gods Varuna and Mitra (though more closely associated with Mitra); the couple would be reflected in Rome by <i>Terminus</i> and <i>Iuventas</i>. Aryaman is the god of young soldiers. The function of <i>Iuventas</i> is to protect the <i>iuvenes</i> (the <i>novi togati</i> of the year, who are required to offer a sacrifice to Jupiter on the Capitol)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[268]</a></sup> and the Roman soldiers (a function later attributed to Juno). King Servius Tullius, in reforming the Roman social organisation, required that every adolescent offer a coin to the goddess of youth upon entering adulthood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[269]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Dumézil's analysis, the function of <i>Iuventas</i> (the personification of youth), was to control the entrance of young men into society and protect them until they reach the age of <i>iuvenes</i> or <i>iuniores</i> (i.e. of serving the state as soldiers).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[270]</a></sup>
A temple to <i>Iuventas</i> was promised in 207 BC by consul <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Livius_Salinator" title="Marcus Livius Salinator">Marcus Livius Salinator</a> and dedicated in 191 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[271]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Penates">Penates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Penates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penates" title="Penates">Penates</a></div>
