<p>The Romans considered the Penates as the gods to whom they owed their own existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[272]</a></sup> As noted by Wissowa <i>Penates</i> is an adjective, meaning "those of or from the <i>penus</i>" the innermost part, most hidden recess;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[273]</a></sup> Dumézil though refuses Wissowa's interpretation of <i>penus</i> as the storeroom of a household. As a nation the Romans honoured the <i>Penates publici</i>: Dionysius calls them <i>Trojan gods</i> as they were absorbed into the Trojan legend. They had a temple in Rome at the foot of the <a href="/wiki/Velian_Hill" title="Velian Hill">Velian Hill</a>, near the Palatine, in which they were represented as a couple of male youth. They were honoured every year by the new consuls before entering office at <a href="/wiki/Lavinium" title="Lavinium">Lavinium</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[274]</a></sup> because the Romans believed the Penates of that town were identical to their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[275]</a></sup>
</p><p>The concept of <i>di Penates</i> is more defined in Etruria: <a href="/wiki/Arnobius" title="Arnobius">Arnobius</a> (citing a Caesius) states that the Etruscan Penates were named Fortuna, Ceres, Genius Iovialis and Pales; according to <a href="/wiki/Nigidius_Figulus" title="Nigidius Figulus">Nigidius Figulus</a>, they included those of Jupiter, of Neptune, of the infernal gods and of mortal men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[276]</a></sup> According to Varro the Penates reside in the recesses of Heaven and are called <i>Consentes</i> and <i>Complices</i> by the Etruscans because they rise and set together, are twelve in number and their names are unknown, six male and six females and are the cousellors and masters of Jupiter. Martianus states they are always in agreement among themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[277]</a></sup> While these last gods seem to be the Penates of Jupiter, Jupiter himself along with Juno and Minerva is one of the Penates of man according to some authors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[278]</a></sup>
</p><p>This complex concept is reflected in Martianus Capella's division of heaven, found in Book I of his <i>De Nuptiis Mercurii et Philologiae</i>, which places the <i>Di Consentes Penates</i> in region I with the <i>Favores Opertanei</i>; <i>Ceres</i> and <i>Genius</i> in region V; <i>Pales</i> in region VI; <i>Favor</i> and <i>Genius</i> (again) in region VII; <i>Secundanus Pales</i>, <i>Fortuna</i> and <i>Favor Pastor</i> in region XI. The disposition of these divine entities and their repetition in different locations may be due to the fact that <i>Penates</i> belonging to different categories (of Jupiter in region I, earthly or of mortal men in region V) are intended. <i>Favor(es)</i> may be the <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_language" title="Etruscan language">Etruscan</a> masculine equivalent of <i>Fortuna</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[279]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jupiter_(mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ver_sacrum" title="Ver sacrum">Ver sacrum</a></li></ul>

<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">

All due care would be taken of the animals, but any that died or were stolen before the scheduled sacrifice would count as if already sacrificed. Sacred animals were already assigned to the gods, who ought to protect their own property.</span>

<li id="cite_note-74"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-74">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFWissowa1912">Wissowa (1912)</a>, p. 101: the <i>epula Iovis</i> fell on 13 September and 13 November. The temple foundation and festival dates are 13 September for Jupiter Optimus Maximus, 13 April for Jupiter Victor, 13 June for Jupiter Invictus, and perhaps 13 January for Jupiter Stator<i>.</i></span>
</li>

Kirsopp Michels <i>The Calendar of the Roman Republic</i> 1967 pp. 84–89.</span>

<li id="cite_note-108"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-108">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFWissowa1912">Wissowa (1912)</a>, p. 100, citing Varro LL V 66: "The same peculiarity is revealed even better by the ancient name of Jupiter: since once he was named <i>Diovis</i> and  <i> Diespiter</i>, that is <i>Dies Pater</i> (Day Father); consequently the beings issued from him are named <i>dei</i> (gods), <i>dius</i>  (god), <i>diuum</i> (day)  hence the expressions <i>sub diuo</i> and <i> Dius Fidius</i>. This is why the temple of <i>Dius Fidius</i> has an opening in the roof, in order to allow the view of the <i>diuum</i> i. e.  the <i>caelum</i> sky" tr. by J. Collart quoted by Y. Lehmann below; Paulus p. 71:"<i>dium</i> (the divinised sky), who denotes what is in the open air, outside the roof derives from the name of <i>Iupiter</i>, as well as <i>Dialis</i>, epithet of the flamen of Jupiter and <i>dius</i> that is applied to a hero descended from the race of Jupiter" and 87 M.</span>

<li id="cite_note-164"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-164">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">On the Esquiline lies the <i>sacellum</i> of  <i>Iuppiter Fagutalis</i> (Varro <i>De Lingua Latina</i> V 152 (hereafter LL), Paulus p. 87 M., Pliny <i>Naturalis historia</i> XVI 37 (hereafter NH), CIL VI 452); on the Viminal is known a <i>Iuppiter Viminius</i> (Varro LL V 51, Festus p. 376); a <i>Iuppiter Caelius</i> on the Caelius (CIL VI 334); on the Quirinal the so called <i>Capitolium Vetus</i> (Martial V 22, 4; VII 73, 4). Outside Rome: Iuppiter Latiaris on <i>Mons Albanus</i>, Iuppiter Appenninus (Orelli 1220, CIL VIII 7961 and XI 5803) on the Umbrian Appennines, at Scheggia, on the <a href="/wiki/Via_Flaminia" title="Via Flaminia">Via Flaminia</a>, Iuppiter Poeninus (CIL 6865 ff., cfr. Bernabei <i>Rendiconti della Regia Accademia dei Lincei</i> <b>III</b>, 1887, fascicolo 2, p. 363 ff.) at the Great Saint Bernard Pass, Iuppiter Vesuvius (CIL X 3806), Iuppiter Ciminus (CIL XI 2688); the Sabine Iuppiter Cacunus (CIL IX 4876, VI 371). Outside Italy Iuppiter Culminalis in Noricum and Pannonia (CIL III 3328, 4032, 4115, 5186; Supplememtum 10303, 11673 etc.) as cited by <a href="#CITEREFWissowa1912">Wissowa (1912)</a>, p. 102 and Francesca Cenerini "Scritture di santuari extraurbani tra le Alpi e gli Appennini" in <i><a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9langes_de_l%27%C3%89cole_fran%C3%A7aise_de_Rome" title="Mélanges de l'École française de Rome">Mélanges de l'École française de Rome</a></i> (hereafter <i>MEFRA</i>)  <b>104</b> 1992 1 pp. 94–95.</span>

<li id="cite_note-277"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-277">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Arnobius <i>Adversus nationes</i> III 40. Cf. also Lucan <i>Pharsalia</i> V 696; VII 705; VIII 21.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-278"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-278">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Arnobius <i>Adversus Nationes</i> III 40, 3; Martianus Capella <i>De Nuptiis</i> I 41: "Senatores deorum qui Penates ferebantur Tonantis ipsius quorumque nomina, quoniam publicari secretum caeleste non pertulit, ex eo quod omnia pariter repromittunt, nomen eis consensione perficit".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-279"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-279">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Arnobius <i>Adversus Nationes</i> III 40 4;  Macrobius <i>Saturnalia</i> III 4 9.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-280"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-280">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Gérard Capdeville "Les dieux de Martianus Capella" in <i>Revue de l'histoire des religions</i> <b>213</b> 1996 3 p. 285 citing Carl Olof Thulin <i>Die Götter des Martianus Capella und der Bronzeleber von Piacenza</i> (=RGVV 3. 1) Giessen 1906 pp. 38–39. On the topic see also A. L. Luschi  "Cacu, Fauno e i venti'  in <i>Studi Etruschi</i> <b>57</b> 1991 pp. 105–117.</span>
</li>
</ol></div>
